## User

New session — resuming the Marauders playthrough (Caleb Waddell, 7th-year Ravenclaw, 1977–78).
Before you write anything in-fiction: load the live state from the Supabase
database per PERSISTENCE.md (the database is authoritative — it wins over any
prior narration), and read the handover document I've attached. It covers the
days-before events, the cast, who knows about the unicorn(s), the food, the
forest findings, the gated mysteries, and my standing rules.
Then open the scene: Caleb and Mei waking in the Room of Requirement (the Bone
Zone, Snuggle Charter), the morning of Thursday 15 September 1977. Keep it to a
short waking beat — establish the room, the light, where they are — and hand
control back to me. Don't put Caleb in any posture or decision I haven't chosen,
and don't narrate his interiority.
Honor the gating: record observations, let bounded NPCs be astonished, but never
explain the causes behind the silver water, the gold unicorn, Athena, or the
soul question. Keep the format (▼ header / tight prose / [Tracker] / [Inventory]
/ "Type /? for commands.").
Ready when you've loaded everything.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 07:34
The Bone Zone had kept its winter all night. Snow drifted past the small high window without any hurry, the light behind it gone grey-gold, and the flannel bed held the deep warmth of a long sleep. On the low table the jasmine pot sat gone cold beside two untouched cups and the biscuit plate; the family corkboard watched from the wall, pinned full. Mei was curled close at his back, one arm slung over his middle, her breath slow and even against the nape of his neck — and then not quite even, her hand tightening once where it lay as she surfaced. "Morning," she said, low and unhurried, the word muffled into his shoulder.

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 07:50
Mei made a sound into his shoulder that resolved into "Food," and then, "a great deal of it. You slept like something switched off at the wall." Fresh robes lay folded at the foot of the bed where the room had left them; he got up and pulled them on, and the Bone Zone let them out into the seventh-floor corridor.
The Great Hall was all porridge-steam and grey window-light. They found a stretch of the Ravenclaw bench where Liri already sat, ink on her thumb and a book open beside her plate. "There he is," she said, not looking up, the Cardiff flat under the words. "Two days gone and back like it's nothing. Fixation chapter's due this morning, mind."
His timetable, unfolded beside his plate, laid Thursday out plain: DADA at nine, Alchemy at quarter to eleven, the afternoon free until Care of Magical Creatures at three, and the first Hexfield drill pencilled for six.

## User

read the alchemy chapter

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 08:16
He left the post sealed by his plate and pulled the Alchemy text into the gap between the toast rack and the marmalade. The fixation chapter ran dense and old-fashioned: fixation was the operation by which a volatile body is made to abide the fire — the fugitive thing taught to stay where a cruder heat would only drive it off. Quicksilver was the standing example, coaxed out of its flight into a fixed and biddable salt; and the principle ran the other way as well, the fixed made volatile again, so that no substance's nature was simply given but might be turned toward staying or toward escape.
He read it through. The marmalade went untouched, the post sat unopened, and past the high windows the morning thinned toward nine o'clock.

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 09:08
Henshaw's voice went on at the front, even and unhurried, and Caleb's quill drifted off his notes into the margin. Two unicorns took shape under it with all the artistic conviction of a man who repairs clocks: one standing, four sticks and a horn and a vast lozenge of a body; one lying down, legs folded the wrong way, a wobbling oval. Curled against the back of the lying one, a smaller stick-figure in unmistakable boxer shorts, an arm flung over the beast and a scribbled face of pure contentment. He nudged the parchment sideways onto Cass's half of the desk.
Cass looked down at it. She went still in the particular way she did when a thing refused to compute. "Why," she said, very low, eyes front, "are you in your pants spooning a horse. Why has it got a horn. Why are there two of them." A small breath. "Start at the beginning."

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 10:46
"My eyes are up here," Caleb said.
"My eyes are on whatever you've smuggled into my classroom," Sage said, without inflection, and waited.
He gave it to her straight: the ethanol-Wiggenweld bind, A and C ready to join, the fixation reading he'd done the night before. Since fixation was the morning's subject, this could run alongside it; he wanted her to teach him to do it properly, which was rather the point of the class. He sold the last of it lightly — a real unknown, no answer in any book, which ought to be more interesting than another set exercise. And Cass: not expected to be let in, but the cold ethanol was her hypothesis, and he'd like her to see it run, and to be there if it held.
Sage let the flattery sit, unmoved by it and entirely caught by the thing underneath. "Fixation is precisely today's subject. You've at least chosen your week." She turned to Cass. "If the cold is your idea, Miss Vance, then before you witness anything, you will tell me why I should expect a bind to fix without heat, when three centuries of practice say it will not. Convince me, and you may stay and watch your own argument tested."

## User

.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 10:47
Cass didn't reach for notes. She set her hands flat on the empty bench and gave it to Sage the way she gives everything, clean and without hedging.
"Heat was never doing the binding," she said. "Heat does the extraction. Water's a poor solvent, so three hundred years of brewers cooked the moondew side to force a weak solvent into giving up its virtues. Ethanol's the stronger solvent. It pulls them cold, no fire needed." A small pause, exact. "So the question nobody thought to ask is whether the heat was ever load-bearing for the join itself, or only ever a crutch for water. C takes the heat away and keeps the solvent. If it binds, the fire was three centuries of habit."
Sage's glass rod went still between her fingers.

## User

explain process

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 10:53
He laid it out as he had it. The ethanol halves were finished and waiting; the one unmade piece was the standard hot bark-and-salamander side, so that came first — the tough stuff needed fire to give up its virtue, and it was the slow step besides. He'd brew a single bark side and split it, so A and C drew on the identical half and the moondew side stayed the only thing dividing them. Then join each at the canonical moment, watch whether it took, and put both to a potato cut against the Control.
"Splitting one half is right. Keep the constant constant." Sage's approval was a statement of fact, nothing warmer. "But mind what you're handling. Ethanol is volatile. It hates an open flame, and it hates a boiling half tipped into it. You'll draw the bark side off the heat before it ever meets the ethanol, and you'll make the join well clear of that burner, or you'll bind nothing and lose your eyebrows besides." A beat, the lesson underneath it: "And note what you've built. You're asking a volatile thing to make a fixed one. The instant it takes, or fails to, is your whole answer. Don't blink through it."

## User

*proceed with the procedure

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 10:55
The bark side came first and slow, the way tough things do: wiggentree ground fine, salamander blood, water, fire under it until the brew ran the deep russet a worked bark half is meant to wear. Sage watched without touching, a correction here and there and no more. He drew it off the flame and split it clean in two — one constant, shared.
A first. Off the heat, well clear of the burner, the heated-ethanol half went into its share of bark at the canonical count, and it took: the two folding into one and settling green, a textbook join. Liri breathed out beside him.
Then C. The cold half, no fire anywhere in its history, into the same bark. For a moment it didn't fold — sat layered, two liquids refusing each other — and Cass went rigid. Then it caught, turned all at once the same clean green as the rest, bound and whole and made without heat where heat had always been law.
Sage scored a peeled potato and laid each to a cut. A's closed inside the minute, like the Control. C's closed too, a breath slower, but it closed, skin drawing shut over a cold-bound Wiggenweld that three hundred years of doctrine said could not exist.
"One result is not a finding," Sage said into the quiet. "You'll do it again, and again, and watch it over days before you whisper the word." A pause, something almost warm getting past her. "But it bound, Mr Waddell. Cold. It bound."
Cass hadn't moved, her eyes on the shut skin over the cold-made potion. "It bound," she said, barely aloud, like she was testing whether the words held the way the join had. Then, dry, the crack sealed back over: "I want it on record whose idea the cold was."

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 11:33
He gave her arm a few light double-fisted taps, knuckles barely landing. "Uughh. Purebloods. Always riding the backs of us lessers and stealing the credit."
Cass took the taps without flinching, deadpan. "I resigned the guild Monday, Waddell. Cold ethanol's the first thing I've stolen back on my own account." ## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 11:34
Sage considered it the way she considers anything worth an answer. "For what a magical substance fundamentally is, its nature, what it's made of, how it behaves, what it can be turned into: yes. That is alchemy. Potions tells you what a thing does in a cauldron; Herbology, how it grows. Alchemy asks what it is." A dry beat. "Though identifying is testing, Mr Waddell, not divining. Bring me a material and we find its properties the slow way, by measuring it. I don't read them off it by candlelight." Her eyes rested on him a moment, unhurried, reading the question for the shape of whatever stood behind it, and she let it lie there, unpressed.
Cass had heard the rest of it. "I did a good thing," she repeated, flat, like she was trying the sentence on for fit. It didn't quite sit yet, but she didn't take it off. Then the dryness came back to rescue her. "And if your all-cold D works, and proves the heat was never needed for any of it, then fine. Built on my idea. You can carve it over the door."

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 11:50
He had a flask of silver water in his satchel already, carried since the forest, so the run died before it started; he set it on the bench beside the flowers. The light in it moved slow, the way it does.
Sage took up a flower first. She touched the cold bead at its centre and it drew up after her fingertip in a thin thread, then pulled back whole and left her skin dry; she did it twice more, expressionless, the way she handles a thing she means to remember exactly. Then, at his word, she snapped one bloom from its stem — and the silver in it went out all at once, like a pinched wick, the cut flower left ordinary white in her hand.
She was quiet a moment. "This is not a fixation question yet. It's a properties question. You can't ask whether a thing holds its nature fixed before you know what its nature is, and I don't, nor I'd wager does anyone." Her eyes went to the flask and stayed on the slow silver. "Both of these are new to me, which is not a sentence I often say. But new is a thing we measure, not admire. Bring them to a clear bench and the hours, and we find out what they are the proper way." A dry beat. "Not in the tail of a lesson you've half spent on potatoes."

Sage didn't pretend she hadn't been read. "You've worked out that I'll cross a lawn for a problem I've never met, where I wouldn't cross it for a sandwich," she said. "That isn't flattery, it's accurate, and I dislike being read so cheaply. Fine. The grass, the secret, the ten other things. I'll look. No promise past looking. Plucking-kills-it fits either story. Which it is, is a properties question, and the one I'd settle before I spent a single reagent pinning a light that might be a process."

Body Hair (Temporary): This covers the horse's torso and limbs, shedding seasonally to help regulate body temperature. It grows thicker in the winter for insulation and shorter in the summer. [1, 2, 3, 4]

Mane Hair (Permanent): Located along the top of the neck, this is thick, coarse hair designed to protect the horse against the elements and insects. [1]

Tail Hair (Permanent): The long, strong hair at the end of the spine. Its primary function is to swat away flies, but it is also widely harvested for [Equine Industry] industrial and musical uses. [1, 2, 3, 4, 5]

Tactile Hair (Vibrissae): Specialized sensory hairs found around the muzzle, eyes, and inside the ears. They are heavily supplied with nerve endings and help the horse navigate and judge distances in low light. [1, 2, 3, 4]

Protective / Fringe Hair (Permanent): This includes eyelashes, the forelock (hair hanging over the forehead), and "feathers" (long hair on the lower legs of certain breeds like Clydesdales) to keep debris and moisture away from sensitive areas]*

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 11:55
Caleb set two things in front of her: a stoppered jar holding five fine threads of gold, and a folded cloth opened to five of white — each sorted by kind, coarse mane and long tail, the fine body hair, the stiff little tactile hairs off the muzzle, the soft fringe.
Sage went to the white first, and her face did what a knower's does at something precious but known: unicorn, five clean types, beautifully kept. Then her eyes moved left to the gold, and she stopped the way the others had stopped before her. She did not touch it at once.
"Unicorn hair does not come in gold," she said, flat, as though stating a law she expected the jar to obey. "A foal shades pale-gold and loses it. An adult is white, or it is nothing." A long beat over the five gold threads. "And you have five kinds of it. Which is not a clipping or a fluke. That is a whole grown animal wearing a coat that should not exist."
She looked up, the thirty-years-of-routine wholly gone. "This is what the grass is about. Not the flower."

He drew one white tail hair from the cloth and ran it slow along the edge of a living leaf, root to tip. The hair slid over the silver-lit green and came away a hair — no brighter, no different, a faint cold mineral on it if anything at all. Then he dipped it into the flask. Sage watched without comment until it was finished. "You've learned that a hair gets wet," she said, not unkindly. "Which is the trouble with looking instead of testing. A thing this strange won't show its hand because you waved a thread at it. If it answers to unicorn at all, it'll answer to a clean question on a clean bench, against a control — not to this." A dry beat, and she nodded at the flask. "Stopper that before you lose it to the air. We do this properly, or we don't learn anything true."

He tucked both sets of hair away inside his robes. Sage watched him do it.

Somewhere down the castle, the bell for the end of morning lessons began to ring.

They came back to the classroom rather than the lawn — Mei first with a covered basket, Liri behind her with a second, Cass carrying a stack of cups and the put-upon look of a woman made to carry cups. "Destination unconfirmed," Liri reported. "We followed the food's owner."

Cass stopped walking. Sage stopped where she stood. Neither of them had known there was one still here to see.

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 12:08
He picked four blooms from the nearest tray and brought them across with a jug of her silver water. "These look familiar, Professor?" They were — the same self-lit silver Sage had snapped dark on her bench an hour ago. He laid them by White Lightning's head. She lipped one in, then a second; the silver crushed against her teeth and the cold unfalling bead finally broke and ran clear down her chin.
Then the water. He poured some into his cupped hands. "Here's what gets me. She drinks plain water from a bucket all day. This she wants. You can see she wants it. But she won't take it from a bowl, only from a hand. What property is that? Because I don't know, and I catch myself making answers up." He held his hands to her muzzle and she drank, slow and reverent, eyes half-closing, working every drop from his palms while the full bucket sat ignored at her side.
A last little into his palm, touched to the spiral of her horn. It did not run off. It climbed the grooves, the cold light sinking up into the bone until none of it showed. "And that. What's that?"
Sage said, very quietly, "I have no idea. And I have spent thirty years being the woman in the room who has an idea." She did not look away from the horn. "Show me that one again."

"She's a unicorn. She should be wary of most people — that's just unicorn, not this." He poured a little silver into his hand and laid his palm flat against the spiral of her horn. "This is the part I mean."
It did the thing again. The silver didn't run off down the bone; it went the wrong way, climbing the grooves of the spiral against any sense of how water ought to move, the cold light drawn up into the horn until his palm came away dry and the silver was simply gone — into her.
Sage had moved in close, near enough to watch the light travel. She didn't speak until it had finished. "You're right to separate them. A frightened animal I understand. That, I do not." She straightened slowly. "Water does not climb. Light is not a liquid to be drunk. I have watched both happen twice now and I have nothing — no mechanism, not a guess I would defend out loud." A breath, and the dryness gave way to something plainer. "I'll help. Not as your teacher. Because I want to know."

"The best kind of trap," Sage agreed, and sat down on the dry grass at the edge of the straw in her good robes — which, from her, was a small surrender. "I've not eaten out of doors since I was a girl. Tell no one."

From the straw, Imogen had the grace to say nothing at all this time.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 14:25
They worked it like people who'd done a day's labour before. Hagrid took the heavy cuts with the saw; Caleb severed and limbed the smaller stuff with a clean Diffindo; Mei sorted as they went, stacking, binding, labelling. Athena hung high over the canopy the whole while, and twice Caleb felt her sweep the wood and find nothing worth a warning — trees, a fox, the far line of the creek, no spiders, no trouble.
Birch first: three good limbs off a coppiced stand, bark bright and papery. Then the rowan, and here he was fussy, building two piles — the softer outer browse-bark and limb for White Lightning's feed, and a separate set of clean, close-grained heartwood and inner bark he liked the look of for potions and for his own wand-wood reading. Mei tied off each bundle so they'd never muddle.
Then grass: an armful of mixed forage from the sunny break where the canopy opened, and a hunt along the damp draw for the particular grass he'd meant to dry, a fat green bundle of it gathered once he turned it up.
By half-past they had a proper load. Hagrid shrank the lot with a few economical taps and Caleb pocketed it, light as a handful of twigs. The slant of the light said it was time to turn for the castle.
Haul (green weight, all shrunk to carry):

Birch (feed-wood, limbs + bark) — 9.0 kg
Rowan/wiggentree, eating-quality (browse-bark + limb, for feed) — 4.5 kg
Rowan/wiggentree, potions-quality (clean heartwood + inner bark) — 2.5 kg
Mixed forage grasses (fresh) — 3.0 kg
[your named grass], for drying (fresh) — 1.5 kg
Total — 20.5 kg

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 14:42
He shook the haul back to full size in the grass — the green bundles, a fistful of pale rowan bark-strips — and carried White Lightning a double handful: meadow grass in one fist, silverweed in the other, the strips laid over the top.
She went for the bark first, the way she always did, lipping the strips off his palm and working them with that slow sideways grind; then the grass, unhurried, sorting it the way grazers do, taking what she liked and letting the rest fall. She stood easy enough to eat, careful of the splinted leg.
The rest of the grass he spread across the warm flat top of a hay bale and the sunny ground beside it, turned so it would dry even — silverweed and meadow both, going pale and fragrant in the heat.
Then he sat back down, put his arm round Mei, and let the afternoon do what it liked with the next quarter of an hour. Mei made a small contented sound against his shoulder and didn't say anything at all.

He dug the three out of his pocket and set them in her lap. Mei thumbed the first seal and settled against him, her voice low and easy over the grass.
"Hexfield reply. From a Mr Dunstan Pyke." A smile came into it as she read. "'Delighted to hear of a new game with proper rules and proper care taken, the more so for it being the young who've built it. I kept goal for an Ilkley side in my day and there's nothing like a sport for keeping a head on straight. Find two Galleons enclosed toward your colours, and put me down to come and watch the first proper match. We could all do with somewhere to cheer just now.'" She shook two coins out into his palm. "He sent the two Galleons."
The second she read more quietly. "Mrs Cordelia Ashdown. 'I don't doubt the merit of it. But I'll own I'm careful these days about whose name sits next to mine on a list, and who signs it. Tell me who stands behind the thing — staff, school — and I'll think again. No offence to you. It's the times that are rude, not me.'"
Neither of them said anything for a moment. Even the cheerful letters had the war folded into them now.
"And the last is your parson." The postmark was a Lincolnshire one, the hand careful and old. She read it slow.
"'Dear Mr Waddell and Miss Chang — yours was a pleasure; we get few letters that care for the old fabric. There is a worn memorial set low in the south wall, the lettering all but gone. I have squinted at it myself and would not swear to more than a few strokes — though an Æ at the start is not impossible. As to the pre-Reformation chest: the parish lost it long before my time. What survived of the early registers went up to the county record office at Lincoln in my predecessor's day, and what did not survive, did not. If ever you are in Lindsey you would be most welcome to come and make of the wall what younger eyes can. — Yours in the parish of St Cuthbert, Whitlock.'"
She folded it back along its creases and didn't ask. White Lightning shifted in the straw; somewhere up the slope the first bell for afternoon lessons would not be long.
[Narrator note: the parson's reply is bounded to what a country clergyman would plausibly know of his own church — a worn stone, a chest long since dispersed to Lincoln. The Aedith Wulfricsdohter thread reaches past that, toward something the campaign hasn't yet committed to as canon. If you want to develop what's actually under that south wall, send me the shape of it in a separate chat and I'll build it properly; otherwise I'll keep it to what's locally, plausibly there.]

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:01
The Hufflepuff boy got it — something about the eggs running hot enough to catch dry tinder, an Ashwinder trait bred down the line — and Kettleburn beamed and gave Gryffindor a point, or Hufflepuff, he wasn't fussy which house this early in term.

A couple of the others snorted. The Newt-Drake settled lower in its straw, ridge-plates clicking flat, and watched Caleb come with one slow sideways eye.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:01
"It's not a familiar. Don't be mad. Just a normal owl, from — somewhere." Athena, on his shoulder, did not dignify the slander. Caleb folded down cross-legged into the grass directly in front of the crate, an arm's length from the open lid. "And — bites like a stapler? That doesn't sound so bad. What am I missing?"
Kettleburn's eyes lit with the particular joy of a man whose trap has been walked into freely. "What you're missing, Mr Waddell, is that a stapler bites and staples." He crouched beside him, delighted, lowering his voice for the whole class to lean in. "Two rows of recurved teeth, and a little gland behind them weeps a resin that sets in about four seconds. So it doesn't tear and let go. It clamps, and it glues, and then it sits on your hand getting comfortable while you try to think of a charm that loosens tree-sap without taking your skin with it."
The Newt-Drake, as if on cue, opened its mouth in a slow unbothered yawn — two neat rows of backward hooks, a bead of amber welling at the gumline — and shut it again with a soft, sticky clop.
"Still keen to sit at the front?" Kettleburn asked pleasantly.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:02
"I think your stapler wants servicing," Caleb told him. "But no, I'm staying right here. Not approaching. Awaiting instruction, or attack, whichever you're offering."
"Sensible boy. Mostly." Kettleburn straightened, addressing the ring. "Then observe, and tell me when. I'm going to tap the box — once, soft — and I want someone to call the exact moment it decides it's threatened. Not when it moves. Before it moves. The tell."
He reached out and rapped a knuckle on the crate's side.
The Newt-Drake didn't lunge. For a second it did nothing at all — and then, low and quick, the ridge of plates along its spine lifted a fraction and went still, and the slow sideways eye stopped sliding and fixed, flat and forward, on the nearest warm thing. Which was Caleb's knee.
"There," Kettleburn said softly, pleased. "Did anyone catch it? The plates, then the eye. That's your half-second. What does a body do with it, Waddell — you've front-row seats?"

## User

"Arresto Momentum"

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:02
"Arresto Momentum."
The wand came up smooth and the charm landed clean — not on the Newt-Drake, but on the air just in front of its snout, a hanging pocket of slowed time. The plates were already up, the eye already fixed; it struck for his knee on the back half of that half-second, low and fast —
— and hit the charm. The lunge dragged, its blunt head ploughing forward through air gone thick as syrup, jaws closing on nothing a full beat too late, amber string trailing slow from its teeth. It looked, for a moment, almost embarrassed.
Kettleburn made a noise of pure delight. "Ha! Did you see — he didn't shield, he didn't flinch, he put the slow on the gap it had to cross." He clapped Caleb on the shoulder hard enough to rock him, sending Athena half off in an affronted flap. "Reflex and the right charm for it. That's the Hexfield boy, that is — five points, and the rest of you write down what he just did, because that's the lesson and he taught it for me."
The Newt-Drake, released as the charm thinned, pulled its head back into the straw with enormous wounded dignity and refused to look at anyone.

.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:03
The rest of the class did as they were told, quills scratching, a Ravenclaw girl muttering slow the gap, not the thing to herself as she wrote — which Caleb was fairly sure was going to end up in three different essays by Friday.
Kettleburn coaxed the Newt-Drake the rest of the way out for them, looping a padded snare-pole gently behind its forelimbs so it could be lifted without grudge, narrating the whole time: where to hold it, where never to, how the resin gland sat soft and warm under the jaw and how you could feel it tense a breath before it fired. He let two of the braver students lay a gloved hand along its armoured back. It tolerated this with the long-suffering air of a creature being petted by amateurs.
"Right — that's the dangerous bit done, and nobody's glued to anybody," Kettleburn announced, well pleased with the world. "Next: what it eats, which is where it gets properly strange. Anyone ever fed a thing that only takes its food on fire?"
Athena, on Caleb's shoulder, gave the crate one last contemptuous look and began, pointedly, to preen.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:03
The string was still hanging where the Newt-Drake had left it, a thread of amber going tacky by the second. Caleb had his wand up before it could drop. "Wingardium Leviosa."
The thread lifted, drawing out fine as spun sugar — and then it set, exactly as Kettleburn had promised, hardening in the air into a thin curl of amber with a bead at its end. Four seconds, spit to solid.
Kettleburn watched him float it and barked a laugh. "Oh, you're that sort. Good. We keep too few." He dug a small stoppered specimen vial from a coat pocket that seemed to hold a great many things and lobbed it over. "Go on. Mind it doesn't touch your fingers wet, that's the whole lesson; set, it's harmless. Half the apothecaries pay for Newt-Drake resin, mind — sets clean under water, which ordinary glue won't. A decent little curio you've got there."
Caleb lowered the amber curl into the vial and stoppered it. The Newt-Drake watched its own spit get pocketed and looked deeply unimpressed with the whole arrangement.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:04
"Two good questions, and one of them keeps your fingers," Kettleburn said.
"The hardening — it's the air that sets it, like a Freezing Charm hits water. Keep the air off and it stays workable: the apothecaries draw it straight under a film of oil, or hold it in stasis. Cold buys you time as well. A chilling charm on it and you've minutes instead of seconds." He tapped the vial in Caleb's hand. "That one's already gone, mind. You'd want to catch the next one wet and cold to keep it soft."
"As for feeding him —" he fished a pair of long iron tongs and a strip of fatty meat from his bottomless coat — "never the hand, which you'd half-decided to test for us. He eats only on fire. So." He pinched the meat in the tongs, touched his wand to it — it caught with a soft whump — and held the burning morsel out at arm's length. "Lit, and well away from you. He comes to the flame, not to you. Want to offer it, since you're so fond of the front row?"

## User

"So presumably.. you're saying not only does he clamp on, then he sets me on fire? are they drawn to the fire? like, if I set a pile of straw on fire?" I take the tongs and hold the meat down.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:04
"Worse than that," Kettleburn said cheerfully. "He doesn't set you alight. He doesn't have to. He clamps, he glues, and then he just waits — the resin holds him on while he chews at his leisure. The burning's how he likes his food going in, not a weapon. Small mercy."
The meat caught and burned in the tongs as Caleb lowered it. The Newt-Drake's whole demeanour changed — the sour patience dropped away and it came forward off its plates, drawn straight at the flame, jaws working, and took the burning morsel clean off the end of the tongs with a snap and a satisfied huff of smoke through its nostrils.
"And no," Kettleburn added, watching Caleb's face do the thing it was doing. "Don't get ideas about the straw. They're drawn to a held flame, a feeding-sized flame — light a whole bale and you don't bait him, you panic him, and a panicked one fires resin at anything warm and tries to climb it. Which is exactly why" — he gestured at the dry straw of the paddock, the lesson clicking shut — "we never, ever keep him near a field he could set going. You feed the fire small, in your hand's reach, and you put it out yourself."

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:06
When Kettleburn turned them loose to work in pairs — sketching posture, calling the tells — Caleb did his quietly, off to the side, where it wasn't a performance.

Then the resin. Wingardium Leviosa lifted it clear before it could touch a thing; a breathed Glacius frosted it and held it soft past the second it should have hardened; into the freed vial it went, and a film of the oil poured over the top to shut the air off entirely.
Workable, sealed, cold. He stoppered it. The Newt-Drake huffed a curl of smoke, robbed twice now and fully aware of it.

He crouched back to the creature's level, easy now, and talked to it the way he'd talk to anything he'd decided to love a little.
"Hey. Listen. You still get to eat. You didn't even have to use the spit — look, it's on fire, it's dead, it's not going anywhere. We're both happy. Fair trade, yeah? Don't sulk."
The Newt-Drake regarded him with one flat amber eye, ridge-plates settled, smoke leaking thin from its nostrils. It did not warm to him the way the unicorn had — it was a sour, ancient, gluey little thing and it owed him nothing — but it stopped sulking. It shuffled forward, took the next burning scrap off the tongs with marginally less spite, and let its blunt head sit a half-inch nearer his knee than before. From a creature like this, that was practically a love letter.
A few feet off, a Slytherin boy muttered, "He's negotiating with the lizard," and Kettleburn, passing, said happily, "He's doing better with it than you are, Mr Avery — write that down too."

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:08
He fetched another strip from Kettleburn's tin and crouched back down, talking low and steady. "Here. Look. Don't waste your energy — don't spit, don't lunge. I'm bringing it to you ready to eat."
"Incendio." The meat caught in the tongs, a clean small flame at arm's length.
The Newt-Drake didn't lunge. It had no reason to now — the food came lit and offered, the way it liked, no work and no fight. It came forward off its plates, unhurried, and took the burning scrap neatly off the end, jaws closing on flame and meat with a contented hiss of smoke. No string welled this time; nothing to clamp, nothing to glue. It simply ate.
"That," said Kettleburn, who had drifted back to watch, "is the whole of good creature-keeping, and most people never learn it. You stopped making it afraid. A thing that isn't frightened doesn't bite." He looked genuinely pleased, a little wistful. "Take care it's the only lizard you spoil, eh. We've only the one tin of meat."

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:09
So he worked the room. As each pair baited theirs and a fresh string welled, Caleb drifted over — a quiet may I here, a nod there — lifted the wet amber clear with a flick before it could set, chilled it, and dropped it into the same vial as the rest. The level rose string by string, the oil already on top floating up to cap each new addition, so the air never touched any of it. No need to pour more; the seal kept itself.
By the time the pairs had run out of meat he had a vial half-full of soft, cold, workable resin under a clean film of oil — a proper little reserve, gathered off a dozen sulking lizards who'd each have glued someone's hand to a fencepost given the chance.
Kettleburn watched the last collection with frank approval. "You've turned my dangerous-beasts lesson into a harvest, Waddell. Half my apprentices never think to keep what a creature gives off." He didn't sound like he minded at all. "Five more, and I'll want to know what you do with it."

"That's the spirit," Kettleburn said, beaming. "First one's only ever the core. The good stuff's in the ones that don't work."
The lesson ran on toward its end. ## User

I want to go and look at the whomping willow from a distance

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:16
He drifted away from the dispersing class and across the grounds to the far slope where the Willow stood — and stopped a good way off, well outside the reach of it, the way everyone learned to in first week or regretted not.
From here it looked almost ordinary. A big old tree, thick-trunked and gnarled, leaves going autumn-gold, standing alone in a wide ring of bare scuffed earth where nothing else dared grow. Still. Patient. Younger than it looked — it hadn't been there when the oldest students started.
Then a rook made the mistake of settling on an outer branch. The whole tree convulsed — limbs lashing up and over in one vicious whip-crack, the air snapping with it, the bird gone in a burst of feathers and indignation — and just as fast the Willow folded back into stillness, leaves resettling, as though it had never moved at all.
On his shoulder, Athena declined entirely to comment.

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:21
Greenhouse Three was warm and green and smelled of wet earth and something faintly medicinal. Sprout was elbow-deep in a trough of compost when he came in — gloves to the wrist, a smear of soil on one cheek — and looked up without surprise. "Mr Waddell. And the owl. Wipe your feet, the pair of you."
Down the near bench the Merdrake glasses stood in their neat matched row, seedlings hanging pale-rooted in the water, the dark-arm glass tucked under its little hood. Further along, three of his night-flowers sat hooded in their own pots, the cloth glowing faintly silver at the seams where the light leaked through.
She peeled off one glove and reached for an ever-present mug of tea gone half-cold. "You've the look of a lad who's come about something — or come to hide from something. Which is it today?"

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:22
"Both, always." She set the mug down. "Glad she's eating. Don't tell me the cookie recipe — I'll only want to improve it." Then, brightening at the actual question the way she did at any good plant problem: "The Willow, though. Now there's a proper one."
She leaned on the bench. "Whomping willow — rare, vicious, and worth a small fortune, which is half why nobody bothers to guard that one; it guards itself. Yours out there's a youngster, six years planted, still finding its temper. The thing to know about the species is they've every one got a still-point: a knot, low on the trunk. Press it — a long pole, from well outside the reach — and the whole tree goes slack as a cut string. Dead still. That's the only way anyone safe ever works one. Without it, you're the rook."
She eyed him over the compost, shrewd and not remotely fooled. "But you didn't walk down here for tree-care, did you. You want a piece of one to build a wand round."

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 15:23
"It's not a timer — that's where you've got it wrong," Sprout said, kindly. "Press the knot and the tree's slack for as long as the knot stays pressed. A switch, not a countdown. The danger isn't five seconds; it's that the moment your hand leaves that knot, it wakes up furious and it's faster than you. So nobody works one alone. One holds the still-point, one does the cutting, and you've a clear way out before either of you lets go."
She tipped her head at the rest of it. "And yes — a willow won't miss a finger. Most forgiving wood there is for cutting; take a slender outer whip clean and it'll have grown three more by spring. The tree's unbothered. You're the fragile party in that arrangement."
A dry look over the compost. "Whether the wood'll do anything wound round a gold unicorn hair, I can't tell you. I grow them; I don't marry them to a core. That's Ollivander's art — and you've already said his name. Go and watch his face when you set a gold hair on his counter."

A glove waved at the hooded pots. "Your rorilucens are holding. Rooted, lit, cold at the bead, perfectly content to glow in the dark and tell me nothing. But they're not dying on me — which, for a thing I can't name, is the most I'll promise you."

Imogen's whole face lit. This was clearly the question she'd been put on earth to answer.

He drew the willow whip from his pocket — forearm-long, pale at the cut — laid it across his palm and tapped it. "Reducio." It folded down to the size of a matchstick. He tucked it away, hauled himself up out of the root-gap, and stepped past the slack tree onto open lawn where Mei stood waiting.
Behind them, Imogen backed off to the far end of her pole, gave the wedge one sharp sideways knock, and snatched her hands clear. The Willow came awake all at once — a shudder, then the whole crown whipping round at empty air where they'd just been, the root-gap gone under thrashing branches as if it had never opened. She scooped up the pole on the retreat.
Caleb walked away across the grass, Mei falling into step at his shoulder. The afternoon was going long and gold behind the castle, and the tree raged itself quiet at their backs.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 16:18
Mei caught the handoff at once. "Go on, I'll walk Imogen back and fill her in on the haunted house." A quick squeeze of his hand, a look that said don't do anything clever without me, and the two of them peeled off toward the lean-to, Imogen already talking with her hands.
The third floor was near-empty at this hour. The humpbacked one-eyed witch stood in her usual alcove, grey stone worn smooth, hump high on her shoulder, that single carved eye fixed on nothing. Up close she was unremarkable — a statue people walked past a thousand times. No seam he could see, no handle, no give to a casual press on the hump or the base. Just old stone keeping its own counsel. Whatever opened her, if anything did, it didn't announce itself to a boy standing in front of it looking hopeful.
Behind him the corridor stayed quiet — a portrait dozing, the long light going amber through a window.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 16:18
He put a finger to the single carved eye and pressed. Nothing. Just cold worn stone, the socket shallow and solid, a fleck of old grit coming away under his nail. He pressed harder, worked his thumb round the rim of it — no give, no click, no seam parting anywhere down the length of her. The witch went on staring past him at the empty corridor exactly as she'd stared for a few hundred years, wholly unbothered by being poked in the eye.
Whatever woke her, it wasn't that.
The corridor stayed quiet around him — a portrait snoring two alcoves down, the light gone amber and low through the far window, the castle settling toward evening.

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 16:20
"Open? Oh, aye." A wet, comfortable chuckle. "More than once, over the years. Not often, mind, and never the same lot for long — they grow up and go, and some new clever sort finds her." He scratched at his chins. "Trouble is, I'm here and she's all the way down there, and the ones who know her don't stand about announcing the trick. Backs to me, every time. A bit of fuss — a touch, a word I never once caught at this distance — and she'd yawn herself open and swallow them whole. Shut again before I'd blinked."
He spread his hands. "Fifty years I've watched it, and I couldn't tell you how it's done. The ones who manage it knew before they ever came — somebody told them. It's not a thing you'll poke out of her, lad." A sly tilt of the head. "Though I'll say this much: they came back smelling of sugar, more often than not. Make of that what you will."

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 17:28
He worked the book the other way, too — against the creatures he actually knew, hunting for what could be taken without a wound.
Feathers, mostly. Owls, hippogriffs, the odd augurey: a moulted feather costs the bird nothing, and the Owlery floor was thick with them — quills, fletching, small lesser magics, Athena's own grey down included. Then a line that stopped him: thestral tail-hair, named a wand-core of rare and difficult power, mastered only by a wizard the creature would suffer — and cut from the living beast doing it no harm at all. Hogwarts had thestrals; everyone knew the carriage-horses nobody could see. Whether he could was another matter.
A pattern settled out of it. The materials you took kindly were always the same kinds — hair off a tail, a feather let go, a thing a creature gave you, drool gone to resin in a vial. The dear ones, the ones in the roped paragraphs — horn, blood, hide — were the ones that wanted a death.
Which made Cawley's flat no horn from the living sit oddly under his eye, because he knew better than the book did. A unicorn could give you horn — a peeler-thin curl off the outside, weeping at the little pores, nothing deeper — if it chose to. Pennycuik had told him so. Cawley only knew the men who took it by force, and those men only ever had a corpse. The book had no page for a gift.

The lesson had taken. Even Crewe stayed home in her hoops, glaring at her own goalposts like they might wander off again.
Round two ran on, louder and smarter, the board ticking awareness marks now instead of penalties.

She was looking down at him over the length of herself, gone very pink and very still, the Welsh died on her lips mid-word — caught between a laugh and something that wasn't a laugh at all. Her mouth opened. Nothing came out. One eyebrow tried to climb into an expression of withering judgement and got about halfway before her composure abandoned the attempt completely.
"You," she managed, low and uneven, "are a deeply unserious man." But she didn't pull her foot back.
In her glass, Gwenllian had gone tactfully, pointedly silent — the particular quiet of an old woman finding something extremely interesting about the far wall.
Across the pool, without opening her eyes, Mei said serenely: "There it is."

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 19:02
Liri took her turn with the same focus she gave everything — unhurried, curious, learning him by degrees, the way she'd learned Welsh archives and Alchemy benches: thoroughly, and with quiet delight when something worked. Imogen surfaced from her contented sprawl enough to be kissed, and to kiss back, finding she wanted that much and saying so — but stayed in the warm shallows of it, her own edge already found and honoured, happy to be held.
No one reached past what they'd each said yes to. The boundaries held themselves, soft and clear: Liri eager and in it, Mei the open centre, Imogen received and resting. Four people who'd had a brutal few days, warm and unhurried and exactly where they'd chosen to be.
Eventually the heat and the long day caught them, and the pace gentled all on its own toward stillness.

Greenhouse Three was black and dripping, the glass loud with night rain. Muffs on — the glasses were Silenced, but Sprout's rule was muffs always, and Caleb kept it.
He checked the light arms first. Glass 2, the slime arm: water gone properly green and murky, a slick of algae furring the root — and the root growing anyway, fat and pale in its soup. Left untouched; the muck is the experiment now. Glass 3, the clean arm: a faint green haze had crept back up the waterline since the last cleaning. He pointed his wand — Evanesco — and lifted the film and slime away clean, water only, not a drop disturbed. Bright glass, sharp root, reset.
Then Glass 1. The pending one. In the full dark he lifted the tarp just enough, one careful look. The water sat clear — no algae at all, nothing for it to feed on without light — the little root pale and blind-white in there, growing about as well as its sunlit siblings. "She loved it," Caleb confirmed, shucking his damp outer robe and reaching for the folded pyjamas with his name on them. "Made happy noises I have never heard a unicorn make. Smelled it like fine wine. Fell asleep in the middle of her own bath." He climbed onto the absurd wide bed and flopped. "And then, because we are professionals, we blanketed her and serpentined away."
"Serpentined," Imogen repeated, delighted.
"Low. Fast. Crouched. Like the SAS." He demonstrated with two fingers zig-zagging across the duvet. "Cannot have a unicorn knowing your true exit pattern."
Liri pulled the blanket back to make room, dry as ever. "You absolute lunatic." But she said it the way you say come here, and shifted into his side as he settled, and Mei climbed in on his other flank, and Imogen tucked along the edge where she'd always said she'd be.
Four sets of pyjamas. One ridiculous bed. The snow window going soft and silent. Athena a small dark shape dozing on the headboard. Outside, the war; in here, four tired people who'd had a brutal week, warm and accounted for and exactly where they'd chosen to be.

MASTER_MERPHILA_GWENLLIAN_SHRINE replacement entry

# MASTER_MERPHILA_GWENLLIAN_SHRINE

## Current status

The shrine is awake.

The glass-panel figure Caleb called Merphila is a bound watching/listening portion of **Gwenllian ferch Rhydderch**, a Welsh hedgewitch from Llanuwchllyn whose craft was laying unresting/snared souls to rest. The rest of her soul is sealed in a small Hywel-made glass flask beneath the deep-end tile of the prefects’ bathroom shrine.

## Names

- **Gwenllian ferch Rhydderch** — true name, recovered and spoken.
- **Merphila** — Caleb’s kind placeholder for the glass mermaid/panel function, from mer + philos.
- **Annwyl / Annwyl Bevan** — deprecated drift / stale placeholder; do not use as current canon.

## Origin

Hywel Bevan, Gwenllian’s son and a master-glazier, built the prefects’ bathing-room and small adjoining work in 1671–72. Unable to let his mother go, he bound part of her into the glass as a watching/gatekeeping presence and sealed the rest in a glass flask under the deep-end tile.

## The two waking keys

1. **Name** — Gwenllian ferch Rhydderch spoken correctly in Welsh.
2. **Pitch** — the 13C sustained bell-tone, tuned through cold-lake + dew water.

Caleb held 13C. Liri Penrose spoke the Welsh name on the tone. The water took the note and tuned it true. The shrine woke.

## Three substantives

- **Dew** feeds / strengthens a thinned snagged soul.
- **Night** hears / listens.
- **Path-water** goes / walks the soul out.

## Gwenllian’s craft

Chosen ghosts are not her work. Snagged souls are: those who did not choose and cannot leave, caught by fear, anger, violence, speed, or confusion. Gwenllian’s work is to find them, strengthen them, hear them, and walk them to the door they could not find.

She calls it a kind of midwifery at the other end of life.

## The deep-end tile

The tile does not hide a literal death-door or ordinary passage. It opens to a shallow rock recess holding a sealed Hywel-made glass flask, glowing with a low dew-gold: the kept/rest portion of Gwenllian’s soul. Caleb saw it, did not move it, resealed it, and left the decision for Gwenllian’s wishes.

## Access

There are two keyed shrine doors known to Caleb:

1. **Grand Staircase lake-window tile** — a charmed floor-tile at the foot of Hywel’s lake-window. Caleb’s hand passes through, finds a catch, and opens the keeper’s stair down to the shrine.
2. **Merphila/Gwenllian panel mark** — star-over-drop mark at the panel, opens the keeper’s stair from shrine side.

The Grand Staircase window also functions as a doorbell/contact point.

## Witnesses to waking

Mei Chang, Aisling Doyle, Mairi Gow, Brigid Connolly, and Liri Penrose were present. Brigid’s MY ASS master-sheet role matters.

## McGonagall disclosure

On Monday 12 Sept, Caleb and Mei disclosed the soul-flask and Hywel/Gwenllian truth to McGonagall. McGonagall’s building-rolls confirm Hywel Bevan, master-glazier, 1671–72. Caleb did not move the flask. The private Grand Staircase passage was not disclosed in that cited tracker.

## Open threads

- Gwenllian’s wishes: rest vs record / teaching before release.
- Caleb’s intent: preserve/record her craft if she wishes, help her pass on if she chooses.
- Salve of Memory / Three-Sleeps / asphodel safety remains gated by McGonagall and adult oversight.
- Do not open soul-door equivalents from curiosity.
Suggested DB operation plan

If the DB supports record edits:

Create/upsert MASTER_MERPHILA_GWENLLIAN_SHRINE.
Update MASTER_SCONCES shrine section with a redirect:
“Superseded by MASTER_MERPHILA_GWENLLIAN_SHRINE for shrine identity/state.”
“Use PROJECT_MY_ASS_SCONCE_AUDIT for sconce rows.”
Update PROJECT_MY_ASS_SCONCE_AUDIT with 13C current wording if missing.

Annwyl,
door-under-tile,
glazier-corridor-door,
stale Merphila only gatekeeper nodes.

Merphila,

Add retrieval warning to stale docs:
“This is pre-waking / pre-retcon. Do not use for current canon without MASTER_MERPHILA.”
Minimal correction prompt for Claude / DB agent

Use this if asking Claude or another DB agent to apply the fix:

Repair Merphila/Gwenllian shrine canon. Supersede stale MASTER_SCONCES shrine material. Current canon: true name is Gwenllian ferch Rhydderch; Merphila is Caleb’s placeholder for the glass-panel function, named by Caleb, not self-named. Merphila/Gwenllian is a bound portion of Gwenllian’s soul in glass; Hywel Bevan, master-glazier and her son, bound part into the glass and sealed the rest in a Hywel-made flask beneath the deep-end tile. The tile does not hide a literal death door or passage. It hides the flask. The shrine woke when Caleb held 13C and Liri spoke Gwenllian’s name in Welsh through cold-lake + dew water, tuning the pitch true. There are two keyed access doors: Grand Staircase lake-window floor tile keyed to Caleb, and shrine-side star-over-drop panel mark. Glazier corridor-door is deprecated drift. Gwenllian’s craft is laying snagged/unresting souls to rest, not binding them. Dew feeds, night hears, path-water walks/goes. McGonagall knows the soul-flask and Hywel/Gwenllian truth from Monday 12 Sept disclosure and building rolls, but the private Grand Staircase passage was not disclosed in that cited tracker. Merge sconce mechanics into PROJECT_MY_ASS_SCONCE_AUDIT; keep 13C as shrine-critical pitch sconce. Mark Annwyl/Annwyl Bevan as deprecated drift/placeholder, not current canon.

## Narrator

▼ Thursday, 15 September 1977 — 22:34

The Bone Zone had made itself sensible: one absurd wide bed, a banked fire, the snow-window gone to a slow dark drift. They'd talked low for a while — the unicorn, the drill, Imogen still occasionally muttering *foot cult* into a pillow — and then, one by one, the talking thinned to breathing.

Caleb lay in the middle of it, Mei's head on his chest, Liri a warm weight along one side with her ledger finally abandoned on the floor, Imogen curled at the edge where she'd always said she'd be. Athena dozed on the headboard, one eye opening now and then to check the count of them and finding it right.

Outside, the war went on being patient. In here, four tired people slept, warm and accounted for, and the castle held its quiet around them until morning.

The day is flushed and the four are asleep. On the cleanup:

**Deleted six rows** that were actively stating things now known to be false — the real retrieval poison:
- *"Mei's orientation not established"* (she's a participant — that retraction now contradicts canon)
- *"Caleb/Imogen platonic that night, doesn't know her answer"* (the stale framing that made me mislabel her tonight)
- the *"Annwyl should not have been spoken"* retcon (its only effect was to keep injecting the dead placeholder name)
- the *flask retcon* row that restated the dead "door under the tile" version
- the wrong *18:50 shy-Liri* beat and its *18:51 correction* (the re-run is the live version)

The current truth for every one of those is preserved — either in a clean later row, or in `MASTER_MERPHILA.md` / `MASTER_MY_ASS_SCONCES.md`, plus the full transcripts as backstop. Nothing load-bearing was lost.

**Added one clean day-close** — a single current-state digest (relationships as they actually stand, what's new, open threads) so a session-load grabs one authoritative row instead of reconstructing the night from twenty fragments.

**The rule from here on:** when something gets retconned or corrected, I *overwrite or delete the stale row* — I do not stack an "addendum" on top of a wrong one. Corrections stop being archaeology.

Two things I deliberately *didn't* touch tonight, because doing them blind at end-of-session risks shaving real canon: a handful of genuine **duplicate** rows from early September (same event logged twice), and a few **war-reveal correction pairs** from the 12th where the wrong and right versions both still sit in the log. If you want, I'll do a dedicated consolidation pass on those — reading each pair in full and collapsing it to one clean row — as its own task rather than rushed in here.

Sleep well. Friday's got Flitwick, a unicorn who needs turning, and an axe to give back to Hagrid.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 06:52

Morning came to the Bone Zone the way the room had decided morning ought to — no hard sun, just a slow grey-gold seeping through the snow-window and a fire burned down to embers that still knew to keep the chill off. Athena was already up on the headboard, turning a slow, disapproving look over the four of them for the crime of sleeping past dawn.

Mei was half-propped on one elbow, hair everywhere, watching him with the unhurried fondness of someone who'd been awake a few minutes and hadn't yet wanted to move. Liri lay dead-weight along his other side, gone boneless the way only the truly off-duty manage. Imogen had migrated in the night from the edge to somewhere near the middle — a fact she would, later, absolutely deny.

"Morning," Mei said, low. "You've a unicorn to turn before lessons, and I've got Flitwick to charm into two books. The day already has opinions about us."

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 07:23

"You are my day," he told her, and kissed her, and got up to make good on the rest of it.

The Bone Zone, asked nicely, gave him a corner kitchen — a little iron range, a good pan, everything to hand. Imogen's camera came off its strap and onto a stack of books facing the bed, wound to a timer she couldn't have predicted if she'd tried. Then bacon, and the smell of it doing what bacon does to a sleepy room: sausages, fried eggs with the lace gone crisp at the edges, black pudding, grilled tomato and mushroom, beans, fried bread, the lot. A pot of Earl Grey steeped strong, then split four ways with steamed milk and a whisper of vanilla — four London Fogs, pale and fragrant, one per person.

He carried the whole groaning tray back to bed and climbed in among them. Three sleepy faces, four plates, the fog steaming. Somewhere on the books, unbidden, the shutter went *click* — catching them mid-laugh, Imogen's fork already in someone else's egg.

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 07:31

The photo had developed by the time he reached for it — and it *moved*, the way wizard photographs do: a four-second loop caught and replaying. Mei mid-laugh with her London Fog halfway to her mouth. Liri caught looking at Caleb instead of the camera, then catching herself and looking away, then back. Imogen, gloriously, with her fork buried in what was unmistakably Mei's egg, frozen in the act and then — every loop — glancing up guilty and grinning. Athena a small cross blur on the headboard. The whole frame warm and gold and unstaged.

He passed it round. Mei snorted into her tea. Liri went a bit pink at her own face doing that. Imogen took one look at her egg-thievery immortalised and said, "Oh, that's *staying.* That is the best thing that has ever happened. Do not redo it. I'll fight you."

"Generally speaking," Caleb said, leaning across to fork a mushroom and a corner of sausage off Imogen's plate with the ease of a man who'd decided her plate was a commons, "*redo* means somebody doesn't like it for a reason — which means I've probably got a reason to *like* it. Imogen looked betrayed by the entire bed. Then she stole his fried bread in retaliation, and the matter was, for the moment, settled.

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 07:34

"Mei," Caleb said, with the gravity of a man about to unveil a Rembrandt, "with your permission — since they've seen everything anyway — could I show them the one picture I consider my greatest work of art?"

Mei didn't even look up from her London Fog. "The mustache." It wasn't a question. A beat, then a slow, resigned smile. "Go on. But if either of them frames it I'm revoking darkroom privileges for life."

He hung the breakfast photo on the corkboard, then plucked a smaller frame from the cluster and turned it round.

In it: Mei, asleep, and arranged with painstaking care across her lower belly — a magnificent curling handlebar mustache, fashioned entirely from white and gold unicorn hair. The loop caught the exact second her eyes opened: blank, then registering, then the dawning, scandalised, helpless laughter as she understood what he'd done — with two of the rarest materials on the planet — purely for the bit.

Imogen made a sound like a kettle. "That's — Caleb, that's *unicorn hair* — do you have any idea — that's a *Healer's annual salary* arranged into a — " She gave up and wheezed.

Liri just stared, reverent. "It's beautiful. I hate that it's beautiful."

"Right. *Department of Magical Games and Sports,*" he read, "*Office of Sports Recognition.*" A beat. "It's signed by one E. Tolley, who sounds tired."

He read the meat of it aloud, walking: the Department acknowledged his inquiry into the recognition of a new game, *Hexfield.* Recognition of a wholly new sport, Mr Tolley regretted to say, was not a thing done by return of owl — it wanted a written rulebook lodged with the Office, evidence the game could be played *without sending children to St Mungo's,* a named adult or institution willing to stand behind it on the record, and, customarily, a demonstration before a Department representative. Novelty, the letter allowed, was *looked upon with interest, though with the caution the present times oblige.*

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 08:11

He stayed at her head the whole way and talked to her like she was a person, because as far as he was concerned she was. One hand flat on the broad plane of her cheek, the other smoothing the line of her jaw, and a low steady stream of nonsense — *good girl, clever girl, I know, I know, same as last time, hay and a wash at the end of it.* She leaned her weight into his palm and breathed warm against his wrist and let him.

"Now," he said softly, and the three wands lifted together.

*Wingardium Leviosa* — gentle, balanced between them, Imogen's whole attention on the splint as the legs came up and the great body rolled slow through the arc of her back and down onto the clean side. White Lightning made one low complaining sound and then was over, blinking, resettled. While she was up they whisked the soiled straw out and forked fresh underneath. Then the warm dew-water, the sponge working down the long-hidden flank — and the same thing happened as last night: the brace, the shudder, the melt. She sighed into it. By the end the new-down side was clean, both sides of her gleaming, and she dropped her muzzle into the cucumber-sweet water and just breathed.

"That," Imogen said quietly, hands finally easing off the splint, "went perfectly."

Just a horse's mouth, on a creature that was anything but.

Imogen watched the whole thing with her chin on her knees, smiling. "She trusts you to put your hand *in her mouth.* Do you understand how mad that is."

So they went up — the long climb to the seventh floor, the south-west turret stair, the sleeping-tabby portrait of Solomon at the foot of it. Caleb gave the catch its phrase — *we have come from the bust* — and the painting yawned, stretched, and swung aside.

The office took them in the way it always did: fire already lit low, the long window this morning showing a grey Welsh hillside under fast cloud — Liri's, unmistakably, the room reading whose feet had just crossed the threshold. She stopped dead, looked at it, and said nothing for a moment.

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 08:21

He went to the bench where the two cold steeps sat under their cooling charm — **D** and **E**, the all-cold experiments — and lifted each crock to the window light in turn.

**D**, the everything-in-one-jar brew, was a disappointment to look at: a thin, washed-out green, more pondwater than potion, the ground bark sitting sullen at the bottom having given up almost none of its colour to the cold. Nowhere near.

**E**, the two-halves version, told a split story. Its ethanol-and-moondew half was already clear and faintly silver-green — looking, honestly, close to right. But its cold bark-and-salamander half was the same weak pale wash as D: barely tinted, the bark stubborn.

"Huh," Liri said, leaning over from her desk. "The ethanol side's basically there and the bark side's barely started. Both jars are waiting on the *bark.*" She didn't push it further than the observation. "Not a colour you'd join yet, either of them."

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 08:22

"Scratch what I said about a lagging side, then," Caleb said, holding D and E back up to the light with fresh eyes. All ethanol, both of them — and it showed. The spirit was pulling colour out of the cold bark that water never would without a flame under it: both crocks were coming up a real, deepening green, D muddier for being one combined jar, E's two ethanol steeps each cleaner and clearer. Not the bright Control-green yet — still deepening — but visibly *working,* cold, which was the whole point. A colour-call for another day or two, not today.

He set them back under the cooling charm and turned to the bench. "B. Right — remind myself." He found its label and read it off. "B's the same build as A: standard hot bark-and-salamander side, moondew-and-mint side in ethanol — *but* B's ethanol half gets brought up to brewing temperature and held about ninety seconds before the join. It's the *does warming the ethanol help the bind* test."

He frowned at the empty space where B should be. "Except — B isn't made. I never prepared its heated half, and there's no hot bark side done either. There's nothing of B to look at. It's still just a label."

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 08:24

He pulled Baruffio's onto the desk, found his place in chapter two, and settled in to read while the fire ticked and Liri's quill scratched across the room.

The chapter was on enunciation as a carrier of intent — Baruffio's central, much-mocked thesis that the syllable is not a password but a vessel, that a charm fails not because a sound is *wrong* but because the wand-hand and the speaking-mind disagree about what is being asked for. The famous buffalo anecdote sat in its box on the page, primly footnoted. Beneath it, drier and more interesting, a passage on why duelists who narrate their own spells aloud tend to cast slower than those who have stopped needing to.

Time passed in the warm quiet. The hillside in the window dimmed and brightened as the painted cloud moved.

## User

[[OOC: I do not think that you have pulled correctly what the book I am reading is about or what it is supposed to be teacing me]]

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 09:06

*Baruffio on the Defended Mind* was not a comfortable read, which was rather its point. Chapter two moved off the first chapter's principles and into the actual discipline of occlumency — and Baruffio's central, contrarian claim came clear: the mind cannot be made *blank*. The blank mind was a duellist's fairy tale. A legilimens met with emptiness simply knew at once that emptiness was being performed, and pushed harder. What could be done — the only thing that could be done — was to *order* the house, and decide which rooms stood open.

The chapter walked it slowly. Strong feeling, Baruffio held, was the handhold: grief, fear, want, anything hot left a surface an intruder could climb. The work was not to feel nothing but to set the hot things behind ordinary doors and keep an unremarkable front room furnished and lit for any caller. The beginner's drill at the chapter's end was unglamorous — each night, before sleep, walk one's own surface thoughts and notice, without judgement, which were loud.

The fire ticked. Liri's quill went on across the room. Outside the window the painted Welsh cloud kept moving, and the morning ran quietly down toward Potions.

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 09:07

He turned the page into the chapter's closing note. Baruffio, having spent forty pages insisting the mind could only be ordered and never emptied, allowed himself one dry aside: that the people who most needed the discipline were precisely those who believed they had nothing to hide, since an unguarded honest mind was simply a house with all its doors propped open and a fire lit in every room.

The line sat there on the page, waiting to be taken however it wanted taking.

On the bench the cold steeps sat greening under their charm; the Ministry letter waited by the inkpot. Not quite half nine. Time yet before the dungeons.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 09:12

He laid it out for her plainly first — the bond with Mei that runs both ways, sending and receiving, the quieter familiar-line to Athena — and then the ask: cross-legged, knees almost touching, would she let him try to push a surface thought across to her, and try to catch one back, and would she do the same to him. They settled. Knees touching. The office quiet, the fire low.

He reached — and it was like pressing a palm flat against a window with nothing on the other side he could grip. He held a single simple thing and pushed it at her.

Liri's brow twitched. "Something," she said slowly, eyes shut. "Not a — not a picture. Pressure. Like you're *leaning* on me. I can't read what." A pause. "Try again, softer."

When they swapped — him reaching to catch — what came back wasn't words or images either. It was the *texture* of her: focused, lit-up, a thread of nervy delight under the concentration, the surface of her attention turned full toward him. No content. Just the shape of her, warm and close, and a dull ache starting behind his own eyes for the effort of holding the reach.

On the third pass something near-landed — a single clean note of *cold-bright-green*, gone before he could be sure he hadn't made it up — and Liri's eyes snapped open. "I held a colour that time," she said, breathless. "Did you — what did you get?"

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 09:16

The alchemist took over from the girl with the nosebleed. Liri tucked the tissue away and actually thought about it.

"Depends what you're asking the hair to tell you. The quick standard one's a conduction read — how cleanly it carries a charge of magic, measured against a known control. That's the fast assay: once Sage's kit's out, maybe forty minutes to an hour of real bench time, and you'd get a clean number." She tilted her head. "Willingness — how the material *wants* to behave, its temper — that's slower and fussier. Half a day to do honestly, and it's more art than number at the end."

She wiped her nose once more, checked it, satisfied. "But you know what she'd say. One test is a *direction,* not a finding. If it were me I'd run a white hair and a gold one through the same test in the same session, so the comparison actually means something — and I'd want her bench, and probably her nod, given those hairs are half hers now." A dry little smile. "An hour buys you an honest first look. It doesn't buy you an answer."

## User

"so the hairs dont get used up for her tests?"

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 09:17

Liri shook her head. "Depends on the test, but the conduction read? No — it's non-destructive. You clamp a single hair between two contacts, run a measured charge through, read how cleanly it carries against the control. The hair comes off the rig exactly as it went on. You can run the same strand a dozen times." She thumbed toward where the collaborative set sat with Sage. "That's why she keeps a working set out — they're reusable for the standard assays."

A small qualifier. "Some tests *do* consume — anything that burns, dissolves, or grinds the material to read what's inside it. Ash-content, full chemical breakdown, that sort. You'd sacrifice a strand or two for those, deliberately, and you'd pick your worst-looking hair to do it. But conduction, willingness, basic temper — all non-destructive. You get those for free, as many times as patience allows." She tilted her head. "So a first session costs you nothing but bench time. Which, given how many you're holding, is the sane place to start."

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 09:42

He woke the shop and worked methodically, the way his father's bench had taught him.

The four to be split first — two birch, two rowan — ripped clean down their length on the fine saw, each opening into two matched halves, eight in all, kept carefully paired to their partners. On the flat of every half he ran the small router: a shallow channel down the centre, narrowing as it travelled, a core-bed that tapered toward where each tip would be.

Then the four to be bored — two birch, two rowan. He trimmed a quarter-inch off each fat end and set the little end-discs aside. He mounted each blank to spin true and fed a long, narrow bit down the dead centre, slow and patient, stopping a quarter-inch short of the tip and letting the bit leave the bore faintly tapered. Keeping a thirteen-inch channel running true was the finicky part; three came out dead-centre, the fourth wandered a hair off true near the base — usable, noted.

Eight wand-bodies now, in two geometries, the end-discs saved for caps. What they'd do once a core went in was another matter entirely — and a thing no book on the rack would tell him.

The eight bodies are racked and logged — four to be glued, four to be capped, the saved end-discs set by. What goes in them, and whether any of it takes, is still all ahead of you.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 09:48

Two rowan bodies, two methods.

Into the first, he laid three white body-hairs down the routed channel, dead centre, settling them into the taper. He took the Newt-Drake resin from its chilled bottle — kept frozen to hold off the air-set — and ran a thin bead along the channel and the mating face, wetting the hairs where they lay. He closed the matched half down over them, lined the seam true, and clamped it the length of the wand. It would harden iron-hard as it warmed and met the air; that one needed time under the clamp.

Into the second, three more white body-hairs, the same careful seating. No resin this time — he laid the dry halves together, clamped them true, and said *"Reparo."*

The seam drew itself shut. The two cut faces knitted as if the rip had never been made, the grain running unbroken across the line, the hairs sealed somewhere inside solid rowan. He took the clamp off and it was one whole piece — seamless to the eye, seamless under his thumb.

Two cored rowan blanks, then: one glued and curing, one annealed whole, three white body-hairs sealed in each. Whether anything had *taken* — whether these were wands or only pretty cored sticks — there was no telling but to try one, and he hadn't.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 09:52

He worked the two built rowan wands over with a fine paper first, then a finer one still — long, even strokes down the grain, knocking back the last roughness of the lathe and the faint ridge where each had been closed.

The resin-bonded one, Wand A, still wore a hairline seam down its length, a paler thread where the join sat; the sanding smoothed it flush but didn't erase it — the glue-line would always be faintly there to a careful eye. Wand B, the Reparo-annealed one, took the paper and came up uniform, no seam to find at all, just unbroken rowan warming to a soft sheen under the grit.

Two slim lengths of pale rowan now, smooth to the hand, the heft and balance of wands. Untried, both. The high grits left them wanting only an oil or a wax if he meant to finish them properly.

*I use beeswax on them* *I start my whomping willow branch on the lathe*

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 10:02

He worked beeswax into the two rowan wands with a cloth, the wax going on dull and buffing up to a low, warm sheen. The rowan drank it and came alive — pale honey, the grain surfacing. Wand A's seam showed as the faintest line under the finish; Wand B gave back nothing but unbroken grain. Two finished wands now, whatever else they were.

Then the willow. He brought the shrunk whip back to full size — a green switch of Whomping Willow, still damp at the cut — and mounted it between the centres.

It turned wrong from the first pass. Where the birch and rowan had cut dry and clean, this was wet and stringy, the gouge wanting to tear long fibres instead of peeling curls. And it would not sit dead the way seasoned stock did: it flexed and shivered against the tool, the shavings twitching as they dropped, a green restlessness in it the bench wood never had. He took it down slow to a rough switch of a blank — pale, damp, faintly alive under his hand. Whether green willow would hold true as it dried, or what that liveliness was, there was no telling yet.

The willow's logged, and the two rowan wands are finished and waiting. The bench is yours — Potions in about forty minutes.

He squared up on the nearer dummy, Wand A in hand, his own cherry wand pocketed, and tried a Knockback Jinx.

The wand fought him. Half a second of nothing — a dead beat where the magic should have leapt — and then it came, but wrong: thin, late, sputtering from the tip in a weak grey shove that rocked the dummy an inch on its base and no further. His cherry wand would have flung the thing to the wall.

Something was in it. Not nothing. But it answered the way a stranger answers to a wrong name — reluctant, partial, half-sure it was being spoken to at all. The dummy rocked, settled, barely troubled. The wand sat warm and uneasy in his hand.

The classroom had emptied to its bones: tiered desks, high windows full of grey morning, and the Professor himself atop his customary stack of books at the front, squaring a sheaf of marked essays. Mei had hung back by the dais, satchel on a desk, plainly waiting.

"Mr Waddell." Flitwick peered over, bright and unsurprised. "Miss Chang said you'd come down. We've a deferred conversation to keep — and a second errand she's brought me besides. Two matters, then. Come in, shut the cold out."

Caleb came in and put it to him plainly: where things stood with the Society, and what it took to make the whole sconce-and-old-charmwork business a real, named thing, now that Flitwick had said yes to standing patron.

Flitwick set the essays down and laced his small hands. "Ah. Where we stand is that I am willing — gladly — and willingness is the easy part." A scholar's pleasure crinkled his face. "To sit on the school roster a Society wants three things in the Headmaster's hand: a patron, which you have; a charter saying plainly what you are for and what you will not touch; and a name that can be written down." The twinkle went dry. "Which, I rather suspect, is where you and I shall have our first disagreement."
