Flitwick opened the two packets on the desk under the window-light. The white he knew at a glance — touched a strand with a reverent fingertip, five sorts of it, precious as anything that comes off a living creature.

Then the gold stopped him.

He went still the way the others had. He held a single gold strand up to the grey light, turned it, set it down very carefully, as though it might be a forgery he'd be embarrassed to have admired. "This is *gold.*" Flat, nearly a question. "A unicorn carries gold its first year and sheds it for silver, then white. You cannot gather foal-down — it's gone before the creature's grown. An adult is white, or it is nothing." He looked at the five gold types laid in a row, a whole coat's worth, and did not reach for an explanation. He plainly hadn't one. "And this came off something grown. Which does not happen."

"Bond *essays,*" he added a moment later, with the pained patience of a man who has titled two careful papers and will not hear them called that — Mei, beside him, said nothing and enjoyed it thoroughly. Then, sober: "If you're asking whether the impossible thing in your hand is kin to the impossible thing the two of you *do* — I won't pretend to know. The danger's real, the argument's fair, and a man can't read two at once regardless. You'll have my answer over your storytelling, not before. Now go, the pair of you — Horace will have your hides and I shan't save you."

Mei was already shouldering her satchel, a quick warm cut of a look at Caleb. *Go. I've got the rest of it.*

Logged — noon's set, the books verdict is riding on it, and Mei's got the meeting's tail end. Flitwick's waiting on where to meet, and Slughorn's waiting on the both of you.

Their bench waited, cold cauldron and a fresh-cut salamander vial between the two stools.

He walked them well for a small man, all the way down through the wet grass to Hagrid's pumpkin patch, asking nothing, plainly determined to be surprised. Hagrid straightened from the vines, beaming. And then Flitwick came round the side of the lean-to and stopped as though he'd walked into glass.

In the straw, splinted leg stretched out, coat clean and catching the grey light, White Lightning lifted her head and looked at him.

She would not have taken it from a pail. From his hands she drank, slow and deliberate, plainly wanting it.

He gave her the helpless love of it — both arms round her neck, his face in the clean white of her, a kiss to the velvet of her snout, her warm breath on his collar.

Then he tipped a little silver water onto the spiral of her horn.

It did not run off. It caught, and climbed — threaded up the grooves against every law of falling water, thinning to nothing as the horn drank it in, a faint cold light moving beneath the surface where it went.

Flitwick made a small sound. Forty years of teaching charms, and he reached, openly, for the name of what he was seeing — and did not find one.

Logged — Flitwick's seen the lot and can't name a thread of it, which is exactly right. The water's gone up into her horn, the little professor is undone, and the story's still yours to tell at whatever depth you choose.

The gold one, days before, that had let him near and given hair that oughtn't exist. Then the white one at the foot of a silver spring — starved, broken, freezing — and the whole mad night of it: warming her with his own body, feeding her bark-paste, running half-dressed through the dark for help. He told it as it had happened, mysteries and all — the cold light, the water that climbs — because he had no answers to the mysteries, only the having-seen-them.

Flitwick listened the way a man listens to scripture, the sausage roll forgotten in his fist. When Caleb finished he was quiet a while. "You understand," he said at last, "that there is not a wizard living who holds the whole of what you've just told me. Not Pennycuik. Not me." He looked at her in the straw. "And you carried half of it out of a forest on your back."

Logged — Flitwick has the whole tale now, mysteries intact and unexplained by anyone, and he's named the weight of it plainly. Lunch is mostly crumbs, Fang's working on a sausage roll against your leg, and the little professor's still looking at her like the world just got bigger.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 13:16

He flicked the rowan wand at the front of the room. "Defend yourself from this, if you can."

The spark that left the tip was — small. A single cool orange mote, ambling out across the gap between them at roughly the pace of a drifting ember, giving everyone present ample leisure to watch it come.

Henshaw watched it come. He did not raise his wand. He leaned slightly, almost imperceptibly, to the left, and the spark wandered past his ear and expired against the blackboard with a sound like a moth meeting a window. A faint scorch-dot, no bigger than a full stop, joined the chalk.

"Defended," Henshaw said.

Somewhere behind, Toby lost it. Cass put two fingers to the bridge of her nose. From the side, Lin offered, deadpan, "That's the most dangerous thing you've made all week and it's still losing to a man standing still."

Henshaw set both hands on his desk. "Right. Since Mr Waddell has volunteered himself and his — *artisanal* — instrument as today's demonstration of why wand quality matters under pressure, let's make use of him. Wands away, the rest of you. Waddell, on your feet. We'll see what your morning's work holds when something's actually coming back at you."

Henshaw watched the cowboy routine with the expression of a man who has been teaching teenagers a long time and has, against his better judgement, grown a little fond of this one. He waited for the twirl to finish. He let the pose land.

"Marvellous," he said. "Two wands, neither of which would trouble a sleeping kneazle. ## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 13:18

He crossed the wands and snapped them out at Henshaw in opposing arcs, left and right. "*Incarcerous!*"

The right-hand wand — B, the whole one — answered. A thin cord of rope spat from its tip, honest and willing, and shot the gap. It was simply not very much rope: a single loop's worth, lashing out about waist-high.

The left-hand wand — A, the glued one — coughed. It managed a stub of cord that uncurled a foot from the tip, hung in the air a confused instant, sparked sour at the join, and dropped to the floor in a sad little tangle.

Henshaw moved before the ropes did. One small flick — "*Finite*" — and B's honest loop unravelled to nothing two feet shy of him, mid-flight. He hadn't stepped back. He hadn't needed to.

"There," he said to the room, into the laughter. "*That* is the lesson — and it is not a joke at Mr Waddell's expense, so settle." He nodded at the wands. "One of those is a real wand doing real work badly, and one is fighting its own core. In a corridor, with a real curse coming, the difference between the two of them is the difference between living and not. Wand-craft is not vanity." A dry beat. "Even the gunslinger's. Sit down, Waddell — you've earned the point you made without meaning to make it."

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 13:19

"And for fairness — comparison, not show — my actual, if you'd let me." He slid the two rowan wands into his robes and drew the cherry. Eleven and a quarter inches, springy, unicorn hair, the wand that has answered him since he was eleven. He squared up and threw the same spell. "*Incarcerous!*"

There was no hesitation in it at all. The cherry sang — thick coils of rope erupting from the tip in a single clean rush, crossing the gap in the time the others had taken to cough out a loop, snapping toward Henshaw fast enough and full enough that the difference needed no narration.

Henshaw's wand was already up. "*Protego.*" The shield bloomed; the ropes struck it, splashed wide, and fell coiled and harmless to the floor — but he had moved this time. He had had to.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 13:22

Wands came up around the room. Caleb raised the cherry, fixed on the shimmer of nothing in front of him, shaped the word behind his teeth without letting it past them — *Protego* — and pushed at it the way he'd been learning to push, the bond-muscle, the silent reach.

A shield answered. Thin, a little lopsided, flickering at one edge like a candle in a draught — but *there*, a faint curved sheen of force standing in the air a foot off his wand, conjured without a syllable spoken. It held a second, two, then guttered out.

Not bad for a first honest try. Not the cleanest in the room, either — across the aisle Lin's stood square and steady, no theatrics, because Lin practised things instead of announcing them.

Henshaw, drifting the rows, paused at Caleb's desk a moment. He said nothing about minds; he had no reason to think there was anything to say. "Lopsided. But silent, and real, first go." A short nod. "Again. Smaller word in your head, not louder."

Cass, beside him, had already thrown hers — neat, contained, gone before anyone clocked the effort. She caught him looking. "Practice," she said, "beats commentary." And raised it again.

## User

"protego maxima"

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 13:23

"*Protego maxima.*"

It came out aloud — and that was the first problem, because the drill was silent. The second problem was the spell itself: *Protego Maxima* is the great anchored ward, the one that's meant to span a doorway or hold a room, NEWT-and-beyond work, and Caleb threw it at a desk in a Friday practical with the cherry barely warmed up.

The wand gave him what it could. A broad dome of force bloomed out — far bigger than the little flickering shield before, a real shimmering wall of it — and then, unsupported, far too much asked of one wizard standing flat-footed at a school desk, it shuddered, warped, and collapsed inward with a soft concussive *whump* that ruffled everyone's parchment three rows deep and knocked Toby's quill clean off his table.

Silence. Then scattered, delighted noise.

Henshaw turned from the board very slowly. "Mr Waddell. That was spoken, it was three years above the lesson, and it fell over." A long-suffering breath — under which sat something that wasn't entirely displeased at the *ambition* of it. "It was also a genuinely large ward for a moment, which is the only reason I'm awarding nothing rather than deducting. *Protego.* The small one. Silent. As asked. Before I set you to writing lines about the difference between reach and grasp."

Cass, beside him, retrieved a drifted sheet of her parchment from the floor without comment, and gave him a single sidelong look that said *you absolute child* and, somewhere under it, was laughing.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 13:24

This time he kept his mouth shut.

He set his feet, dropped his eyes half-closed, and reached for it the way he'd been practising in the warm dark with Liri and across the castle with Mei — not the word, the *want* of the word, the bond-muscle flexing — and held the small one in his mind, *Protego*, and pushed it down his arm into the cherry without a breath of sound.

The shield bloomed clean.

No flicker this time, no lopsided edge — a neat curved pane of force standing steady a foot off the wand, even and bright, held while he held it, gone smoothly when he let it go. Better than his first. Quiet as a closed door.

It cost him, a little — a small warm pull behind the eyes, the now-familiar tax of reaching that way. But it stood, and it stood *well*.

Henshaw, two desks down, caught it from the corner of his eye and gave a small approving jerk of the chin without breaking off from the student he was correcting. "Better. That's the one. Again, hold it longer." No remark beyond the craft of it; nothing in his face suggesting he'd seen anything but a boy getting the knack of a silent Shield on the fourth go.

Beside him Cass had clocked the difference, though — the *clean* of it after two messes. She didn't say anything. She just threw hers alongside his, the two shields standing a moment in the air between their desks, and let that be the comment.

She let the pane drop then, on her own terms, and looked at him with the serene, insufferable satisfaction of a woman whose feet had stayed exactly where she put them. The drill was silent, so she said nothing. The single raised eyebrow did all the gloating for her.

A shadow fell across the desk. Henshaw, arms folded, watching the pair of them duel under cover of his drill with an expression pitched exactly between *stop that* and *do go on.*

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 13:26

He kept it up — *Rictusempra,* then snap the wand back to himself, *Protego* — offence to defence, silent, again and again, the switch getting smoother each pass, the tickling-hex jets crossing in bright threads.

She wasn't trying to break him so much as *outlast* him — and his hasty shields were the thing that finally gave, one of them buckling a half-second early under her counter so that a stray tickling-jet of her own slipped through and caught him across the ribs.

His mouth twitched. He clamped down on the laugh before it got loose — barely — and the spasm cost him his next shield entirely.

Cass let her pane drop, victorious and unbearable, and mouthed two silent words at him with relish: *Feet. Still.*

It had been, genuinely, the most fun he'd had with a wand all week.

"Three points to each of you for genuinely excellent non-verbal work — and three from each, for doing it behind my back instead of where I could make the rest of them watch and learn. Net nil. Cass, under her breath, not quite contrite: "Worth it."

Logged — and that one has teeth. How Caleb answers is yours.

He held Caleb's eye.

"So. You're preparing. *Good.* Then let me make sure you prepare to come back from it, and not just toward it. That, I can actually teach."

Henshaw studied him a moment longer, then nodded once — the nod of a man confirming something he'd already half-decided.

Walk me through it properly — how we don't get killed going under that tree, and how we're back before anyone counts heads at supper."

Logged. She's in, near enough — the "all right" is already out of her mouth; what she wants now is the practical spine of it. The unanswered letter just surfaced sideways, which means the *real* reason you're doing this is sitting in the open between you whether either of you names it yet.

She's waiting on your walk-through: the tree, and the clock home.

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 13:54

Cass listened to the whole of it without interrupting — the knot, the long pole, the run, the drop, the blind spot down in the roots where the thing couldn't reach, the reverse with the short stick. By the end something in her had shifted from *humouring him* to *actually planning a crime*.

"You've a *system*." Dry, but the approval underneath was real. "Poke the murder-tree, hold it stunned, take turns being the one who dies if the other lets go. She hitched her bag higher, decided.

Logged. Solve the crossing-the-grounds-unseen problem and you're away.

Your move, Waddell.

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 13:58

The library took him in with its particular hush — afternoon light slanting gold through the high windows, dust turning slow in the beams, the scratch of a few quills the only sound. A scatter of students at the far tables; most of the castle was still in lessons. The restricted shelves stood in their caged gloom at the back, exactly as patient as ever.

Madam Pince looked up from the returns trolley before he'd cleared the second stack — she always knew the weight of his particular footstep by now — and set down the volume she was mending with a thin, assessing patience.

"Mr Waddell." A small dry beat. "Twice this week already, and it isn't even the weekend. Either you've come to actually return something, or you've come to ask me for a thing I'll have to write to someone about." She folded her hands on the desk. "Which is it?"

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 13:59

Pince let both requests sit a moment, the way she let a returned book sit while deciding how badly it had been treated.

Logged. Now she's waiting, both hands flat on the desk, for the one thing she actually wants before she writes anything: *what are you drying, and why the dark way.*

How Caleb answers her is yours.

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:01

"You *assumed* it wouldn't kill you." Pince repeated it the way she'd repeat a due date a student had cheerfully ignored. "What a sentence to say to a librarian." But there was no real heat — and, under the disapproval, the faint reluctant warmth she only ever showed the ones who actually read.

You may not. That door stays shut."

She took the wand he offered almost absently — and then not absently at all. Her fingers closed round the pale rowan; she went still; she turned it once under the lamp, the way Flitwick had, the way a person does when a thing in their hand is not behaving the way the catalogue in their head insists it should.

"...That is a unicorn core," she said slowly, "and it is *wrong.* Not bad-wrong. Wrong like a word in a language I half-know." She looked up at him, sharp. "You made this. With *body* hair." A beat. "Sit down. You're not going anywhere for a moment."

Logged. The willow turned out to be the easy thing — ordinary joinery, open shelf, nothing dark required (and she's slammed the door on the *interesting* kind you've now developed a taste for). But the wand stopped her cold. Pince doesn't go still for much; a unicorn core that reads "wrong like a word in a language I half-know," made by a student, with *body* hair, is exactly the sort of impossibility she'd normally have a catalogue entry to file it under — and doesn't.

She's told you to sit. She's not done with you.

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:02

"A signature for an answer." Pince's mouth did the thing that on a warmer face would have been a smile. "I'd have the parchment drawn by Tuesday. Don't tempt me."

Logged. Pince has handed it back to you with the most useful thing a librarian can give: the honest edge of her own domain. The willow's a solved problem — ordinary seasoning off an open shelf. ▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:03

Pince's eye dropped to Wand A as it touched the desk — the resin one, the faint seam — then came back to him, unimpressed by that one specifically, as though she could feel the difference between the two without casting a thing.

"Here's the suspense spared: saliva is not a storied part. Blood, horn, tail-hair — men have died over those virtues. Spit is in the books only because *someone* always tries the unfamous parts, and the accounts are mostly disappointment and the odd poisoning from what else sat on the creature's tongue." Logged. She's gone to the grille to pull the saliva texts for you — but she's already told you the shape of what's in them: nothing. Spit is the part nobody famous ever bled for, in the books only because curiosity always picks at the unglamorous corners. And on the trade, she's handed you the sharpest piece of advice you'll carry into Slipshod Lane: a wandmaker won't barter for the object, he'll barter for the *knowing* — so the real decision is what you let him see and what stays in your pocket.

She's at the cage, key turning. Wand A's on her desk; B's in your hand. Roughly forty minutes till Mei spills out of Arithmancy.

"Then we agree on the diagnosis," Pince said, "which is more than most of my patients manage." She left him to it, drifting back toward her trolley — close enough to mind the book, far enough to grant a man his reading.

He opened it.

It was thinner than it pretended, and grew thinner still as he read — a catalogue of the *lesser* unicorn matters, the parts no famous wizard ever bled over. Sweat. Tears. The foam of the mouth. Saliva. Entry after entry the same shape: a hopeful experimenter, a claim, and then the author's dry demolition of it — *the subject had also drunk wine; the fever broke on its own; no second account exists.* Saliva got barely two pages, and both concluded the same: no virtue anyone had ever pinned down. One scrap of hedge-lore near the end — that a unicorn's spit *given*, never taken, might sweeten foul water or quiet a sick child — sat under a single flat footnote: *folk belief; untested; included for completeness.*

Nothing. Exactly as she'd promised. He'd put a creature's tongue-water in his mouth on a whim and learned only that he'd learned nothing.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:06

From the trolley, without turning: "Sweet water." A dry page-turn. "Do write to St Mungo's. They'll be glad to know a fever can be quieted by a horse's lick — they've been at it the hard way for centuries."

He flipped back through the thin pages, hunting two things.

Hair first. The author spent one paragraph on it, and that one only to recite the wandmakers' gospel: the *tail* is the sole hair of any virtue, the seat of a unicorn's power; the mane, the body, the fetlock-fringe are *without use* — ornament at best, beneath serious craft. No experiment. No curiosity. The line laid down flat as a law of nature by a man who had plainly never once thought to test it. The book did not know about the wand sitting in Caleb's hand, three body-hairs singing low inside it. It would not have believed him.

Dung and the rest earned a single heading — *Excreta* — beneath which the author allowed only that unicorn droppings were coveted by a certain breed of rose-grower, and that all else claimed of them was peasant superstition unworthy of the page.

Bottom of the lesser unicorn. No sweet water in it for him — only the tidy shape of everything no one had bothered to question.

Logged. The book gave you the wizarding consensus and nothing under it: tail-hair is power, everything else is ornament, and no one writing it down ever once thought to *check* — which is its own quiet joke, given the rowan in your hand humming along on three body-hairs the author would swear couldn't do a thing. Dung's for roses. Saliva's for fools. The lesser unicorn is, by every book Pince can hand you, lesser.

Which leaves the one source that isn't a book — the man you're meeting in Slipshod Lane at three. Roughly forty minutes till Mei's free, the volume's spent, and Wand A's still sitting on Pince's desk.

Pince set the spent volume aside, and for the first time that afternoon looked something other than long-suffering. This was a question she liked.

"You're half-right and half-heretical, which is the interesting place to stand." She leaned on the desk. "The power was never in the wand, Mr Waddell. It's in *you* — always was. A child does magic with no wand at all; a frightened witch can throw a grown wizard across a room bare-handed. The wand doesn't make the magic. It *focuses* it — turns a shout into a sentence. Magic came first. The tool is the refinement."

A second beat. "And no — not only wood. The histories I keep speak of staves, of rods, of foci in bone and metal, older and from elsewhere; cruder things that did cruder work. Wood-and-core is merely the form that won, because it works best in a European hand."

Then the eyebrow went up — the heresy.

"But *wood is just the holder* — there a wandmaker would take your head off. Doctrine holds the wood is no ornament. It has a temper of its own, and it *shapes* what the core gives. The wand is a marriage — wood, core, and the wizard who carries it — not a vial with a label." A small, honest spread of the hands. "*Why* those three make magic where any one alone makes none is precisely what the books don't hold and the guild won't say. A wandmaker's country, not a librarian's."

She drew a slim volume from the trolley and set it before him. "*On Cores: A Comparative Study.* Open shelf, no leave needed. It'll carry your question as far as paper goes. The rest is waiting in Slipshod Lane."

Logged (and your wood-as-holder theory is on the books, classified, unspoiled). Pince gave you the knowable shape of it — power in the wizard, wand as focus, the older cruder foci that lost out to wood-and-core — and then drew the exact line she keeps drawing: the *principles* are hers to lend, the *why* belongs to a craft that doesn't write itself down. She handed you *On Cores* to chew on and pointed you, once again, straight at Slipshod Lane.

You've a slim open-shelf book in front of you and about thirty-five minutes before Mei's out of Arithmancy. Read it here, or pocket it and move?

## User

*I read on cores* "encore"

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:08

*On Cores* was a better book than the last — written by someone who'd actually held the things he wrote about.

It opened comparative. The three great cores, each with a temper of its own: dragon heartstring, the most powerful and the quickest to learn, but proud, accident-prone, willing to bond to a new hand if it lost a duel; phoenix feather, rarest, widest in range, independent to the point of contrariness, slow to attach and never quite *owned*; and unicorn hair — the most faithful of the three, the steadiest, the hardest of any core to turn to the Dark. Its price was plain on the page: the *least powerful*, prone to melancholy, apt to go quiet or "die" outright if badly matched or roughly used.

And — once more, the wall — *tail* hair, always. The author named it without argument, the way the others all did: the tail is the seat; the rest is hairdressing. Not a line on a body-hair core. Not a hint that such a thing could sing at all.

But near the back came something he could actually use: a core has no virtue alone. It must be *matched* to a wood whose temper completes it — a gentle core wants a wood that lends it spine; a wild one wants a wood that disciplines. The pairing, the book said, was the whole of the art.

## User

*look up gentle woods*

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:09

He turned to the wood-tempers — the book carried a pairing appendix, cross-read against *Wand-Woods of Britain*.

The gentle woods sat together. *Willow* — a healing wood, humble, drawn to potential its owner couldn't yet see. *Hazel* — sensitive to a fault, mirroring its wizard's heart, said to weep sap when its owner grieved. *Apple* — for high ideals, ill-suited to Dark work, unhappy in a cruel hand. Mild, all of them. Faithful. Quiet.

Then the appendix's flat warning, which stopped him: pair a gentle wood with a gentle core and you *double* both halves of the bargain. Unicorn hair in willow or apple would make the most loyal wand ever cut — and one of the weakest; a thing that would sooner break than wound. For *power* out of a faithful core, the author said, you do the counter-thing: give it a wood with *spine*. Oak, for strength and fidelity both. Ash, stubborn, clinging to a single hand. Woods that make a gentle core stand up straight instead of lying down.

Rowan — the wood of his own built pair — sat in the protective column: clear-headed, pure-hearted, mighty in defence, notoriously hard to turn Dark.

What wood a *gold-coated* hair might want, the book did not venture. No page on it imagined such a core existed to be paired at all.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:11

**Willow** first. The book warmed to it — a healing wood, and a chooser of the underestimated, favouring the owner with more potential than confidence; quick to nonverbal work and to magic that mends. *A wand for the long road, not the short fight.*

Of the **Whomping Willow** it said almost nothing, and the nothing was loud: a single boxed note that such specimens were *enchanted* trees, not willows in any ordinary sense — singular, violent, propagated by no wandwright, their wood "outside the stock of the craft and the understanding of this book." Whatever his green blank was, the page declined to guess.

Then the two the unicorn cropped from his hand. **Rowan** he half-knew now — the guardian wood, defensive, clear-eyed, an old charm against the Dark made solid. **Birch** the book set beside it as a wood of *beginnings*: light, quick, cleansing, eager, the wood for a young wizard or a fresh start. Nowhere did it remark that a unicorn might choose to *eat* either; that thought was his alone.

**Power**, then. The spine-woods got their full entry. **Oak** — strength and fidelity both, slow to choose, loyal for life, *for the wizard who does not bend*. **Ash** — stubborn, clinging to one true owner, sullen in a borrowed hand. Pair either to unicorn hair and the faithful core gained a backbone it never owned alone. But the book set its ceiling in plain ink: *you may give a unicorn core spine, never fire.* Match it well and it serves faithfully to the end of its strength — and its strength has an end. Power is the marriage and the wizard, never the wood alone.

He closed it. The last thing he wanted wasn't in a wand book at all.

"Madam Pince — other conductors. Not wands. Staves, rods, bone, metal, whatever's carried magic before or instead. Even if it's a book I have to ask for."

She considered him over the glasses — and, to his mild surprise, didn't refuse.

"That's a *history* question, not a craft one, and history I can lend." She moved along the shelf. "*Implements Before the Wand* — staves, rods, the focusing-stones of older and farther traditions. Cruder things, mostly: a staff casts broad and strong and clumsy, two hands for what a wand does with a wrist; metal conducts hot and ungovernable. They lost to the wand because the wand is *precise*, not because they were weak."

She drew it — then paused, one finger on the spine.

"Bone I'll lend you the history of and not one page more. The traditions that worked magic through bone were, more often than not, the ones that worked it through the *dead* — and that shelf wears the same lock as your dark willow. History, yes. Method, no. Are we agreed?"

Logged. The willow gave you healing and humility and a flat refusal to comment on the *Whomping* one — which tells you the only thing the page can: that tree is its own locked door, and not the library's to open. Birch and rowan came up beginnings and guardianship, the literature serenely unaware that anything with a horn has opinions about either. And the power question got its honest answer in iron ink: a faithful core takes a spine but never a flame, and you can love it to the end of a strength that ends.

Now Pince is holding *Implements Before the Wand* half-off the shelf, waiting on one word from you — that you want the *bone* chapter for its history and not its method.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:12

"Agreed." She slid *Implements Before the Wand* across to him, bone chapter and all, on his word.

"And to your question — I meant your *joke*. The dark drying you waved at me an hour ago. Not the tree." A dry flick. "Though now you've made me say it: the Whomping Willow would be a fool's wood to cure by *any* method, dark or patient. Leave that one to people paid to lose fingers."

Both hands settled on the desk. Her voice went level — the register she kept for things she wouldn't dress up.

"And you may stop being *unconvinced*. There are ways to cure wood that are evil, Mr Waddell — not because anyone called them so, but for what they cost. Wood quickened with blood. Grain settled by binding a death into it. Tempers forced into timber that never consented to hold them. They exist, they work, they are exactly as ugly as the word — and your doubting they're real is proof you're decent, which is not the same as proof. You'll not have the *how* of any of them from me." A beat, mild and immovable. "That you find the idea *fascinating* is precisely why."

Then she rose, went two stacks down, and came back with a clothbound book — ordinary, unroped — and set it atop the others.

"*This* is what you actually want. *The Seasoning of Magical Timber.* Kiln-charms, sap-drawing, settling green grain the slow honest way. Open shelf. Take it. Cure your willow like a craftsman, not a ghoul."

Logged. So the "dark willow" was your own joke coming back to bite, not the tree — and Pince settled the wager you didn't quite make: there *is* an evil way to cure wood, several, and she named just enough of their shape (blood, a bound death, timber forced to hold what it never agreed to) to prove the point before slamming the door on the method. The willow you can have honestly — *The Seasoning of Magical Timber* is in your stack now, kiln-charms and patience, the craftsman's road. ## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:13

The dictionary line got a genuine huff out of her — nearly a laugh.

"I keep several. You'd be the first in a month to want one for its purpose." A dry settle. "And yes — a definitions quarrel. *Drying* is what a craftsman does. *Curing by the dark way* is what I refused. You meant the first; I heard the second, because the student who says 'the dark kind most wouldn't venture' rarely means the kiln. My error. Mostly yours."

He read the bone chapter while she talked. History, as promised, and grim: old traditions, far-northern and far-older, that cut foci from bone — of beasts, and where the page turned its face away, of people. Such things conducted strongly and steadily and *wrong*; they were said to keep the temper of the dead thing they came from and bend the bearer toward it. The craft had not so much died as been *put down*. He set it aside.

"Plants like the Willow." She considered. "Sprout's country, not mine — but one reads. Devil's Snare, that strangles in the dark and loosens in the light. Venomous Tentacula, which bites and means it. Snargaluff, all thorn and grab. The Willow's stranger than the lot: violent by enchantment, not by nature, and the only one of its kind — no account I've read agrees how it came to be. Ask Sprout. She'll talk your ear clean off and adore you for it."

A pause, and her voice shifted — phoenixes sat nearer her heart.

"Now *there's* a core worth respect. The bird burns and is reborn from its own ash; its tears close wounds nothing else will; its song puts heart in the honest and dread in the foul. It cannot be *owned* — it stays where it chooses, leaves when it likes. So its feather makes the most *independent* wand there is: widest in range, slowest to give loyalty. A phoenix core will sit cold in a hand it hasn't decided on, sometimes for years. Few are ever given — a phoenix sheds a feather rarely, and only of its own will. Which is why such wands are dear, and the wizard who carries one usually bears a second look."

Logged. The bone chapter gave you exactly what was promised and nothing she wouldn't: history with its face half-turned away, a craft that got *put down* rather than died. And then the warmer half — Devil's Snare and Tentacula and Snargaluff for your dangerous-garden curiosity, with Sprout flagged as the one who'll actually love the question; and phoenixes drawn with real fondness, the core that can't be owned and won't be hurried, cold in the hand until it decides you're worth it.

She's wound down. The afternoon light's moved a hand's width across the table. Roughly half past two is coming, and Mei with it.

## User

"where are they? how does one 'find a phoenix'? I mean, I already have one, Athena. but just like a different person looking for one?"

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:14

"Your owl," Pince said, with a glance at Athena that was pure dry fact, "is an *owl*, Mr Waddell. A fine one. Not a phoenix." She let that sit, untroubled, and took the real question.

"As for finding one — you don't. That's the first thing every account agrees on. A phoenix is not sought; it is *granted*. You cannot trap it, cage it, buy it, breed it, or summon it. It lives wild and far off — the old writers put them in high mountains and hot countries, though half of that is hearsay from men who never saw a feather. What they all agree on is this: a phoenix comes *to a person*. In great need, or to one it has weighed and found worthy. And it stays only as long as it chooses, not an hour past."

A small shrug, and something almost wistful under the dryness.

"So your hypothetical seeker is wasting good boots. The wizards who have a phoenix did not go and *get* one. It came, and it stayed. That is the whole of the method — which is to say, there isn't one."

Logged. Pince's verdict on the phoenix hunt is the bleakest kind of simple: there's no method because there's no hunt. You can't go and *get* one — it weighs you, decides, arrives, and stays exactly as long as it pleases. The seeker with good boots is wasting them. And Athena, for the record and to her dry satisfaction, is an owl.

The light's most of the way across the table now. About thirty-one minutes until Arithmancy lets out and Mei's yours to collect — with three books gathered, a wandmaker waiting at three, and Cass somewhere killing time at the edge of the lawn.

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:15

"More or less, yes." Pince closed the returns ledger. "A phoenix travels by *fire*. It vanishes in a burst of flame and is somewhere else entirely in the same breath — no door stops it, no distance troubles it, and the old accounts hold that walls and wards mean nothing to it. It goes where it intends to be."

She tucked the ledger under the desk.

"Which is the other half of why no one keeps one against its will. There's no room you could build that would hold it a minute past its patience — and no clever seeker can lie in wait, because you cannot ambush a thing that is simply *wherever it has decided to be*."

Logged. Fire and intention — a phoenix is simply *where it means to be*, walls and wards and a hundred miles no obstacle. Which is the whole reason it can't be caught, kept, or stalked: there's no ambush for a thing that isn't subject to distance.

The clock's at quarter past. Mei's loose in thirty minutes, Cass is somewhere at the lawn's edge waiting on three, and you've got your books and a wandmaker's appointment to keep.

## User

*read about magical drying of wood*

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 14:16

*The Seasoning of Magical Timber* was a craftsman's book — plain, practical, the first thing all afternoon that read like his father's workshop instead of a wizard's tower.

The trouble with green wood, it said, was haste. Cut timber is full of sap, and sap leaving *unevenly* is what tears a billet apart: the ends drying faster than the heart, the outside shrinking against a wet core until the grain splits to relieve the strain. The mundane cure was a year on a rack. The magical cures were faster — and the book was stern that faster is not the same as *fast*.

Three honest methods. A **kiln-charm**: a low, even warmth held steady over days, coaxing moisture out slowly; push the heat and you only case-harden the shell over a wet heart. A **drawing-charm** to pull sap from the centre outward — quicker, but apt to leave the wood brittle if run hard. And the **grain-settling** work: seal the end-grain with wax so the billet dries through its faces, then ease the internal tension with a relaxing charm as the water leaves, so the grain settles instead of splitting.

One line sat underscored by some earlier reader's hand: *wood dried in violence keeps no voice.* Force receptive timber and it goes dull — loses the very quality that made it worth cutting.

The book was written for ordinary billets. It had no word at all for a blank that lay, even now in his satchel, faintly green and *lively* in a way no rack would explain.

Logged. That one read like home — sap leaving unevenly is what splits a billet, so you coax it out slow and even (kiln-charm over days), or draw it from the heart outward at the risk of brittleness, and always seal the end-grain and ease the tension so the grain settles instead of tearing. And the line some earlier hand thought worth underlining: *wood dried in violence keeps no voice* — force receptive timber and it goes dull. So you have your honest method for the willow now, gentle and patient, the craftsman's road Pince pointed you down.

The book had nothing for the part of your blank that's still, quietly, *green and alive* in a way no rack accounts for. That answer isn't on a shelf.

Quarter past going on half. Twenty-nine minutes till Mei. Gather up and move, or one more thing here?

The blank lay damp and wholly unbothered in his palm, exactly as green as it had been.

He left the pole lying — no sense wrestling forty feet of it down a hole and onto a chain of Floos — and ran.

He held a half-second longer — then let the stick drop and threw himself after them. "Lumos." His wandtip bloomed white, and the passage took shape — a low earthen throat, roots threading the ceiling, the floor packed and sloping gently up. Cool air, old and still, all clay and time. They walked single file where it pinched, abreast where it gave.

She stopped there, jaw set, having got further than she'd meant to.

Ahead, the passage ran up against a wooden underside — boards, a trapdoor, the dust-and-silence weight of the old Shack pressing down from above.

Logged. The dark and the privacy did what Cass needed it to — she went from deflection ("you leave doors open and don't shove people through them") to the real thing: it isn't the family anymore, that's decided. It's Marcus. She's let the whole shape of her life shift — the cabin, the office, the two of you — and hasn't said one true word to him, and she knows he deserves better than to piece it together by watching her. She got further than she meant to and stopped, jaw set.

The Shack's underside is right above you — boards, a trapdoor, the old silence of the place pressing down. But Cass just put something heavy down in the dark between the three of you, and she's waiting to see what you do with it.

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:11

"Mm." Cass conceded it with her chin, already moving — leading them out of the upper room and down a staircase that complained at every tread. "That's the truer version. Not a butcher. Just two old houses folded into one, and a thousand small dinners where I said nothing — and you're right, you don't stay horrified forever. You get *used* to it. That's how it takes the decent ones. The latch gave with a screech. "Maybe that's the kind thing and the safe thing at once, for once. I'll sit with it — properly, not as a way of never deciding."

She eased the window up onto a grey slice of fenced ground, the rooftops of Hogsmeade leaning beyond.

Logged. That landed the point cleaner than the first pass: not a butcher, a *slope* — the thousand small silences that wear the decent down until they don't flinch anymore. And on "the truth," she heard the opsec in it and took it seriously — Marcus doesn't need names, doesn't need *you two*, not from shame but because it's a loaded wand in the hand of a man who talks. A clean end, no detail, no cruelty: same outcome, less exposure, and maybe — for once — the kind thing and the safe thing being the same thing. She'll sit with it properly, she says, not as a dodge.

The back window's open onto a grey wedge of fenced ground, Hogsmeade's rooftops past it. Mind the drop — and out into the village, with a Floo and London waiting.

He went out feet-first into a tangle of dead nettles — a short, ungraceful drop — and came up in the lee of the Shack's rotten clapboard. Mei landed beside him; Cass came last and drew the window down behind her to its old crooked rest.

Logged. The Three Broomsticks means Rosmerta clocks your faces by supper. The Hog's Head means no one bothers to look.

So: dim yourselves down and change the robes, slip into the Hog's Head where discretion's the house specialty, or some other play entirely?

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:30

Eldower wiped his hands on a rag, unhurried, working out how much to give. He gave it straight.

"Ollivander makes you your first wand. *Picks* you, more like — you stand there, he hands you sticks till one likes the look of you, you pay, you leave glad. Clean trade. He's better at it than I am, and I don't mind saying so." No grudge in it; only fact. "I do the rest. The wand come down from a dead grandmother that never quite answered the grandchild. The one snapped in a fall that the owner can't bear to replace with a stranger. Foreign wood wanting re-housed in something the Ministry won't seize. A core gone tired under a wizard who can't afford to begin again." He dropped the rag on the bench. "Repairs. Re-cores. Re-homing. The cases that don't sit in a velvet tray. People come to me when the wand they've got is *wrong* — and they'd sooner mend the bond than buy out of it."

He looked at the rowan, then at Caleb, and the last of the dismissiveness went out of him.

"I sent you up the Alley because you came in looking like his trade. Then you put *that* on my bench." A short nod at the wand. "So I was wrong. You're not Ollivander's. You're exactly mine."

## User

"if I gave you a core that you've never seen before, would you be able to help me find the right wood for it? to show me how you do that?"

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:31

"Find a wood for a core?" Eldower almost smiled, and didn't quite. "Boy, that's the *whole* of it. That's the craft. Everything else is sawing." He turned the thought over. "And aye — I can show you the doing of it. It isn't a secret recipe. It's a *reading.*"

Then he sobered. "But mind what you're asking, because it changes with what's in your hand. A core I *know* — unicorn tail, dragon, phoenix — I match from forty years of matching its like. I narrow by temper: this wood lends spine, this one humility, this one won't suffer a dark turn. Lore and feel, fast."

A beat.

"A core I've *never* seen — there's no forty years to lean on. No precedent. We wouldn't be looking it up. We'd be finding it out. You set the core against a wood and read whether it *answers* — the same struck note Brigham wrote down — and you go down the rack, wood by wood, till something wakes. Slow. Uncertain. Could run weeks and hand you nothing clean."

He put out a tea-stained palm, open between them.

"But I can't read what I can't hold. You keep saying *if.* Stop saying if. What is it."

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:32

Caleb spilled the lot onto the bench — white in its careful rows, the gold beside it.

Eldower's hand went to the white by habit. "A deal of unicorn. Body hair, same as the wand. Five sorts." Then he saw the gold, and stopped.

He didn't pick it up at first — looked at it the way a man looks at a thing he's been told his whole life is a lie. Then, two careful fingers, he lifted a single strand to the lamp. The shop went very quiet.

"No." Flat. Not denial — a craftsman ruling out every possible and finding nothing left. "Gold is foal-down. Gone before the beast is weaned. You can't *gather* it; there's nothing to gather. And this is a grown coat. Tail. Mane. The lot." He set it down like a live coal. "Forty years I've turned wands. I have never held this. No one living has."

Then, almost against his own will, he took a billet off the rack — birch — and laid the gold strand along it to read the answer.

Something moved. Not a note he knew. Not silence, either. A flicker he had no name for, gone before he could catch it. He drew the hair back as though it had nipped him.

"That is not a Friday job. That's *months.*" Quiet now. "And it's the sort of thing men have cut throats over." The flat eyes lifted, the dismissiveness long gone, something careful and half-afraid behind them. "So before I lay another hand to it — what is it you actually want? A wand off it? Or to know what it *is?*"

## User

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:33

Eldower nodded slowly, like that answer told him more about Caleb than the gold had.

"Then I'll tell you the question, so you can hold it properly." He leaned a hip against the bench. "A wand is the *use* you put a core to. One use. A good one — but you'd be deciding, here, on a Friday, that the whole of this is going to be a stick someone waves. And I don't think you know yet what *this* is, which means you don't know what you'd be spending." He turned a single gold strand under his thumb. "Some cores want to be wands. Some were never meant for it and turn sullen in the wood — and the man who made the wand never finds out, because he only ever asked the one question." A flat look. "You came in saying you wanted to give it the right home. The right home might be a wand. It might not. You don't know, because nobody knows, because there's never been one. That's the question I meant. Not *which wand.* *Whether.*"

He set the strand back with the rest. "Doesn't mean no. Means we'd find out what it is *first,* and let that tell us the home. Slower. Honest." A beat. "Still your call. But now you've got the whole of it."

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:33

"Potions." Eldower said it like he was setting a coin on the counter to look at. "No. I don't brew. And you're right that it's temporary — you grind a thing into a draught and the draught gets drunk and it's gone, and whatever it *was* goes with it." He shook his head. "That's not what I do. Nothing leaves this bench to be used up."

He gathered the gold back into its fold, neat, and pushed it the few inches across the wood to Caleb — giving it back, not keeping it.

"A wand's the opposite of temporary, when it's right. It's the one made thing that outlives the maker *and* the owner and goes on answering somebody not yet born. So yes — if it has a home in it, a wand's a worthy one." A pause, and the honesty again, flat and unhurried. "I only won't promise it's *the* one until I've read it properly, because I'd be lying, and I don't lie about the work. Could be this gold was meant to be a wand and we find the wood that wakes it. Could be it wants to be something nobody's named yet. Either way you'd not be spending it — you'd be *housing* it. That, I'll help with. Gladly."

He nodded at the rowan still on the bench. "Leave me that one a while — the one you made. I'll learn more from your mistakes than your hopes. And come back when you've a day, not ten stolen minutes. This wants daylight and no clock."

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:34

Eldower listened to all of it, and for the first time something near a smile touched his mouth.

"You've the better half right. It isn't *birth.* It's that there's nothing to read — no book worth the name, because the knowing doesn't go on a page. It goes into the hands. At a bench. Slow. Watching a maker and ruining good stock until your fingers learn what your head can't be told." He tapped the wood. "The families say *born to it* because it keeps the door shut and the trade theirs. But you're right — someone stood at a bench first and made it up. It's handed down because it can only be *handed.* Not because it's holy."

He looked at Caleb's hands, then at the wand. "And you've the hands already. Your father's bench, I'd wager. That's the half most never get. What you lack is the reading — and the reading is the *years.* No shortening it, and I'd not trust the man who told you there was."

A flat beat. "So. Aye. I'll teach you. The only way it goes: from the bottom. Plain woods, plain cores, dull work — until your hand can read a *birch* before it ever lays a finger to *that.*" A nod at the gold in Caleb's fold. "You don't start at the miracle. It waits at the *end* of the apprenticeship, not the front. Come when you can give whole days, not stolen minutes." The ghost of dryness. "And come ready to waste good wood. That's the tuition."

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:35

Eldower took the whip first. The moment it sat in his hand his brow drew down — he turned it, flexed it, and it *flexed back,* a faint live spring that seasoned stock simply does not have. He set it down a little quickly.

"That's green. Worse than green — it isn't *dying.*" He weighed the blank Caleb had cured, ran a thumb down the waxed end. "And this one you've seasoned properly. Warming charm, sealed grain, a good hand — I'll grant it. By rights it ought to be going quiet under that. Drying toward still." He frowned at it. "It hasn't. There's a — *pulse* isn't the word for it. A liveliness willow loses the moment you cut it. This hasn't lost it."

He laid both pieces side by side, the cured and the raw, and looked at them the way he'd looked at the gold.

"A wand wants the wood *dead,* understand me. Quiet. The core does the speaking; the wood only holds still and lets it. Living wood — enchanted wood — won't hold still. It *argues.* I'd not guess what that would do with a core in it, because in forty years I've never turned a willow that wouldn't lie down." A flat look, half exasperation, half something a great deal keener. "I told you to start at *birch,* not ten minutes gone. You've put two impossible things on my bench since." A nod at the whip. "Where did you cut this?"

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:36

"The Whomping Willow." Eldower huffed — almost amused, not quite. "That vicious article. A tree bewitched to *move,* to strike, to keep its own counsel." He turned the blank, nodding to himself now. "Then there's your riddle answered. It won't lie down because it was never *made* to. The enchantment isn't *on* the tree — it's *in* it, down the grain. You cut a length off and carried it about, and of course it's still got a pulse. You took a slice of a thing made to be alive and angry."

He set it down with a care that hadn't been there a moment before.

"And you asked Sprout for leave and a method, rather than going at it with a hatchet. Good. Men who poach off an animate tree come back with fewer fingers — if they come back at all."

Then the flat honesty returned. "But knowing *what* it is doesn't tell me what it'd *do.* Enchanted wood is the worst host there is for a core — it won't go quiet and let the core talk; it'll want to talk itself. Might be you can't season the bewitchment out of it at all. Might be that's the whole of what it is." He looked up, and the keenness was unmistakable now. "I'd never turn that for a customer. For a puzzle — cut by the man himself, with permission asked first — *that,* I'd want to watch."

"Now? No." He almost laughed. "You've stolen minutes and an afternoon of errands waiting on you. This is a *day's* work to so much as begin, and a year's to finish — if it finishes."

He turned the blank once more. "Will it dry? I don't know. And that's not me being cagey — I *genuinely* don't, because no one's done it. Maybe never. Maybe the trick isn't drying it at all, but quieting the bewitchment by some other road. Nobody can say, because nobody's tried. That's the whole difference between a puzzle and a job."

Then he set it down and looked at Caleb straight. "But here's the useful part, so take it. You've matched by *romance* — two rare things, surely they're meant for each other. A flat, careful beat. Learn the gold against woods I *understand* first. Let the willow be its own fight, kept separate. One puzzle at a time, lad, or you'll solve neither."

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:38

Eldower took the whip Caleb left without comment and stood it in a corner with stock that was likely older than the boy. He watched the blank go back into the bag and gave a short nod — that one was Caleb's fight to keep charmed.

"A letter." Dry as the shavings. "Of course they want a letter. Schools always do." But he was already wiping his hands and reaching past the bench for a sheet and an ink-pot gone half to skin. "I'll write you true and no more than true — which is exactly what serves you, since true is all you need." A flick of the eyes toward Caleb's bag, and the gold in it. "And I'll keep *that* off the page. The school doesn't need it, and paper travels."

He wrote standing, fast, in a cramped square hand, and turned the sheet for Caleb before the ink had set:

> *To the masters of Hogwarts School —*
>
> *I am G. Eldower, wandwright, of Slipshod Lane, London. I have examined work done by your pupil Caleb Waddell, and find in it a hand worth the training. I am willing to take him as a practical pupil in the craft, on terms to be agreed, should the school see fit to release him for bench-work from time to time.*
>
> *I make the offer plainly, and I mean it. This craft is not had from books — it is learned at the bench or not at all. The boy has the hands. The rest is years, and I would give him the years, if you would give him the days.*
>
> *I ask nothing settled today. Only that the door be opened to the conversation.*
>
> *— G. Eldower*

He blew on it once, folded it twice, and held it out. "There. Proof you're not inventing me." A last flat look, not unkind. "Now go find your apothecary and your chisels before the day's gone. And come back, Waddell — with a *day,* not ten minutes."

They'll not truly turn on one another, and they answer odd in each other's company." A flat beat. "That's the nearest the craft has to bonded — and mind, that's shared *origin.* Not sentiment."

He set the pen down. "Binding two wands *because the wizards are bound* — one made to mirror the other? No. Not that I've heard done. Doesn't mean it can't be. Means it'd be a puzzle nobody's solved yet, and you'd be the one finding out." The keen look again, dry under it. "You've a *gift* for those, seems to me. Go on, then. Apothecary, chisels. ## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:40

Eldower took the sheets, and his brows climbed. He looked a long moment — sliding rings on the one, the cored-out rib-cages on the other — and breathed out slow. "You said *one quick question.*" Dry, not glancing up. "And drawn them up like registry plates with *Ollivander's* name on your own daydreams. That's cheek."

He tapped the first. "This — rings, no glue, *reconfigurable.* Clever engineering that's forgot what a wand *is.* A wand's not an assembly. It's a *marriage* — one wood, one core, settling into each other for years till they're a single thing that trusts you. One you can take apart never settles. Never bonds. You've built an elegant tool that can't fall in love. Five materials in a row isn't a voice — it's a committee."

The second drew something that wasn't scorn. "Worse, as a *wand.* Core slung in a cage, never *touching* the wood — you've put a *gap* where the marriage goes. A core that touches nothing is *alone,* and alone's the opposite of a wand." His thumb stayed on the open ribs. "But that's no wand. That's an *instrument.* A thing for *seeing* a core — reading it, nothing in the way. For learning what a core *is* before you ever house it?" The flat eyes lifted, gone sharp. "*That* might be the cleverest thing on either page. You'd only never hand it to a witch to fight with."

He squared the sheets and held them back out. "Which is a day's talk, not a doorway one. Bring them when you've the day — we'll cut them to pieces proper." A grunt, the ghost of dryness. "Birch first."

Slipshod Lane was browner than before, the upper storeys leaning close, the afternoon thinning toward the hour Caleb had to be back under the tree. Ahead, the mouth of the lane opened onto the bright clatter of the main run — Slug & Jiggers somewhere down it with Mei and Cass inside; Ollivander's narrow window further along; and the light going amber, and the day getting shorter every minute spent standing still.

## User

*go to ollivanders*

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:43

Ollivander's was as Caleb half-remembered from a single morning six years gone — narrow, dim, the air all dust and old wood, thousands of slim boxes stacked to a ceiling lost in shadow. The bell's one note died and nothing replaced it. The shop seemed to hold its breath.

Then a ladder slid along its rail though no hand had touched it, and he was simply *there* behind the counter: pale, soft-footed, eyes the silver of moonlight that did not blink quite often enough.

"Cherry," he said, before Caleb had got a word out. "Unicorn hair. Eleven and a quarter inches — pleasantly springy." The eyes warmed without the rest of him moving at all. "Mr Waddell. I never forget a wand, and that one has served you well — or you'd have come to me sooner." A pause, gentle, and somehow enormous. "And yet here you stand. A man does not call on the maker of a wand that suits him, Mr Waddell. Not without a reason he has been turning over in his mouth a good while before he says it." The faintest tilt of the head. "So. What is it you have brought me?"

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:44

Ollivander's hand rose toward it the way a priest's rises for a relic — and stopped, hovering, not yet touching, as though asking leave of the hair itself.

"May I." It was not quite a question. When Caleb allowed it, he lifted the single gold strand to the dim light on two fingers and, for a long moment, said nothing. The silver eyes did not blink.

"Unicorn," he breathed. "Gold." Softer. "Foal-down is gold, Mr Waddell — and foal-down sheds before the creature is weaned, and cannot be gathered, and is *gone.* This is not down. This is the coat of a grown beast. Tail-length. *Mature.*" He turned it, and the light ran the strand like a struck wire. "Which is to say it is a thing that does not exist. I have made wands a great many years. I have never held its like. I did not believe its like *could* be held."

Then the silver eyes lifted from the hair to Caleb, and *warmed* — and that was somehow the most dangerous thing in the dusty room.

"And you carry a fold of it in your pocket as though it were string." A slow, marveling softness. "Where, Mr Waddell, does a young man *come by* a thing the world agrees cannot be? Tell me — take your time. I should very much like to hear all of it."

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:45

Ollivander did not laugh, did not call it small. If anything the *story* stilled him more than the hair had.

"You tell it as though it were nothing." The silver eyes had not left him. "A unicorn does not *run up* to a man, Mr Waddell. A unicorn flees men. They are shy past all reason of the grown and the male — a wizard your size, your voice, they are *gone* before he has crossed the clearing. And they give their hair to no one. It is *taken,* by gatherers, from the brambles where it sheds." He raised the gold a fraction. "Yet you tell me one walked to you. Let you set a brush to it. That you *asked* — and it answered. Set its horn to your open hand, and went."

A silence. The shop seemed to lean closer.

"That is two impossibilities, Mr Waddell. Not one. The gold — and the *giving.*" His voice had gone soft, and warm, and beneath the warmth sat a hunger he made no effort to hide. "A thing that cannot be, doing a thing it would never do — for *you.*" The faint, unblinking smile. "So I find I am no longer asking only what is in your pocket. I am asking what is in *you.*" A beat. "Tell me — has it come to you only the once?"

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:46

"Only the once." Ollivander repeated it the way one repeats a word in a language one is mourning. "Then what lies in your hand is the whole of it. The only such hair in the world — given once, ungatherable, never to come again." He set the strand down on the black velvet of the counter with enormous care, and looked at it rather than at Caleb. "Finite. Singular. There has never been another. And your *yes* tells me there will not be."

When he raised his eyes the warmth had become something almost tender, and entirely relentless.

"I have made wands a very long time, Mr Waddell — waiting, though I did not know I waited, for a core that had never been made before. Most men live and die and meet only the cores that are in the books." A breath. "You have carried one into my shop. I should be a poor steward of my own craft if I did not say plainly what I am thinking. I should like the *working* of it. I would give a great deal — and I do not mean only galleons, though I mean those too — for the right to be the man who finds out what it wishes to *become.*" The unblinking pause. "Will you let me?"

## Narrator

▼ Friday, 16 September 1977 — 15:46

Ollivander inclined his head, and if the hunger dimmed at all it was only to bank itself — the way a man banks a fire he means to keep alight. "Of course. A thing this singular deserves a slow decision. I am not going anywhere, Mr Waddell. I have been here a very long time, and I am patient past the patience of most living men."

He watched the strand go back into its fold. "But hear one truth before you go, since it was your errand. You will not find the man who can tell you *what it is,* or *why.* I am as near to that man as the world keeps — and I cannot. There is no book with this in it. There never was." The silver eyes held, steady and unblinking. "Whatever it is, you will not be *told* it. You will have to be the one who finds it out." A pause. "Come back when you've decided who you'd trust to help you do that."

The bell let him out into the amber afternoon, and the walk back down the Alley was short. Slug & Jiggers stood where he'd left it, its window of jars catching the low light. Inside, Mei and Cass were two dark shapes near the back shelves, heads bent together over something in a jar; they looked up as the door went. And behind the counter, sleeves pushed back, weighing a grey powder into a twist of paper, stood Cassia — who registered precisely who had come in, and went on weighing.
